<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the first Roman emperor. </tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Augustus" title="Template:Augustus"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Template_talk:Augustus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Template talk:Augustus (page does not exist)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Augustus&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Caesar Augustus</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[a]</a></sup> (23 September 63 BC – 19 August AD 14), also known as <b>Octavian</b>, was officially the first <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Roman emperor</a> who reigned from 27 BC until his death in AD 14.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[b]</a></sup> He is known for being the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Principate" title="Principate">Roman Principate</a>, which is the first phase of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, and Augustus is considered one of the greatest leaders in human history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[3]</a></sup> The reign of Augustus initiated an <a href="/wiki/Roman_imperial_cult" title="Roman imperial cult">imperial cult</a> as well as an era associated with <a href="/wiki/Regional_hegemony" title="Regional hegemony">imperial peace</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Pax_Romana" title="Pax Romana">Pax Romana</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Ara_Pacis" title="Ara Pacis">Pax Augusta</a></i>. The Roman world was largely free from large-scale conflict for more than two centuries  despite continuous wars of imperial expansion on the Empire's frontiers and the year-long civil war known as the "<a href="/wiki/Year_of_the_Four_Emperors" title="Year of the Four Emperors">Year of the Four Emperors</a>" over the imperial succession.
</p><p>Originally named <b>Gaius Octavius</b>, he was born into an old and wealthy <a href="/wiki/Equites" title="Equites">equestrian branch</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plebeian" title="Plebeian">plebeian</a> <a href="/wiki/Octavia_gens" title="Octavia gens"><i>gens</i> Octavia</a>. His maternal great-uncle <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> was <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Julius_Caesar" title="Assassination of Julius Caesar">assassinated</a> in 44 BC and Octavius was named in Caesar's will as his <a href="/wiki/Adoption_in_ancient_Rome" title="Adoption in ancient Rome">adopted</a> son and heir; as a result, he inherited Caesar's name, estate, and the loyalty of his legions. He, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Antony" title="Mark Antony">Mark Antony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aemilius_Lepidus_(triumvir)" title="Marcus Aemilius Lepidus (triumvir)">Marcus Lepidus</a> formed the <a href="/wiki/Second_Triumvirate" title="Second Triumvirate">Second Triumvirate</a> to defeat the assassins of Caesar. Following their victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Philippi" title="Battle of Philippi">Battle of Philippi</a> (42 BC), the Triumvirate divided the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> among themselves and ruled as <i>de facto</i> <a href="/wiki/Roman_dictator" title="Roman dictator">dictators</a>. The Triumvirate was eventually torn apart by the competing ambitions of its members; Lepidus was exiled in 36 BC and Antony was defeated by Octavian at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Actium" title="Battle of Actium">Battle of Actium</a> in 31 BC.
</p><p>After the demise of the Second Triumvirate, Augustus restored the outward façade of the free Republic, with governmental power vested in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Roman Senate</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_magistrate" title="Roman magistrate">executive magistrates</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Roman_assemblies" title="Roman assemblies">legislative assemblies</a>, yet maintained autocratic authority by having the Senate grant him lifetime tenure as <a href="/wiki/Commander-in-chief" title="Commander-in-chief">commander-in-chief</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tribune" title="Tribune">tribune</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_censor" title="Roman censor">censor</a>. A similar ambiguity is seen in his chosen names, the implied rejection of monarchical titles whereby he called himself <i><a href="/wiki/Princeps" title="Princeps">Princeps</a> Civitatis</i> (First Citizen) juxtaposed with his adoption of the ancient title <a href="/wiki/Augustus_(title)" title="Augustus (title)">Augustus</a>.
</p><p>Augustus dramatically enlarged the Empire, annexing <a href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" title="Roman Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia_(Roman_province)" title="Dalmatia (Roman province)">Dalmatia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pannonia" title="Pannonia">Pannonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Noricum" title="Noricum">Noricum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raetia" title="Raetia">Raetia</a>, expanding possessions in <a href="/wiki/Africa_(Roman_province)" title="Africa (Roman province)">Africa</a>, and completing the conquest of <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a>, but suffered a <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Teutoburg_Forest" title="Battle of the Teutoburg Forest">major setback</a> in <a href="/wiki/Germania" title="Germania">Germania</a>. Beyond the frontiers, he secured the Empire with a buffer region of <a href="/wiki/Client_state" title="Client state">client states</a> and made peace with the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a> through diplomacy. He reformed the Roman system of taxation, developed <a href="/wiki/Roman_roads" title="Roman roads">networks of roads</a> with an <a href="/wiki/Cursus_publicus" title="Cursus publicus">official courier system</a>, established a <a href="/wiki/Standing_army" title="Standing army">standing army</a>, established the <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_Guard" title="Praetorian Guard">Praetorian Guard</a>, official <a href="/wiki/Cohortes_urbanae" title="Cohortes urbanae">police</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vigiles" title="Vigiles">fire-fighting services</a> for Rome, and rebuilt much of the city during his reign. Augustus died in AD 14 at the age of 75, probably from natural causes. Persistent rumors, substantiated somewhat by <a href="#Death_and_succession">deaths</a> in the imperial family, have claimed his wife <a href="/wiki/Livia" title="Livia">Livia</a> poisoned him. He was succeeded as emperor by his adopted son <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a>, Livia's son and also former husband of Augustus' only biological daughter <a href="/wiki/Julia_the_Elder" title="Julia the Elder">Julia</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h2>
<p>As a consequence of <a href="/wiki/Roman_naming_conventions" title="Roman naming conventions">Roman customs</a>, society, and personal preference, Augustus (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɔː/: 'au' in 'fraud'">ɔː</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/ʌ/: 'u' in 'cut'">ʌ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">aw-<span style="font-size:90%">GUST</span>-əs</i></a>) was known by many names throughout his life:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Gaius Octavius</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">ok-<span style="font-size:90%">TAY</span>-vee-əs</i></a>, <small>Latin: </small><span class="IPA" lang="la-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Latin" title="Help:IPA/Latin">[ˈɡaːiʊs ɔkˈtaːu̯iʊs]</a></span>). According to <a href="/wiki/Suetonius" title="Suetonius">Suetonius</a>, Octavius added the <a href="/wiki/Cognomen" title="Cognomen">surname</a> <b>Thurinus</b> (<small>Latin: </small><span class="IPA" lang="la-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Latin" title="Help:IPA/Latin">[tʰuːˈriːnʊs]</a></span>) to his birth name as an infant in 60 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[c]</a></sup> Later, after he had taken the name of Caesar, his rival <a href="/wiki/Mark_Antony" title="Mark Antony">Mark Antony</a> referred to him as "Thurinus" in order to belittle him. In response, he merely said he was surprised that "using his old name was thought to be an insult".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsSuetonius12CaesarsAugustushtml5_5]_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsSuetonius12CaesarsAugustushtml5_5]-9">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldsworthy2014_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldsworthy2014-10">[7]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Gaius Julius Caesar Octavianus</b>. He took the name of his <a href="/wiki/Adoption_in_ancient_Rome" title="Adoption in ancient Rome">adoptive</a> father, Julius Caesar, but was often distinguished from him as "Octavianus" (<small>Latin: </small><span class="IPA" lang="la-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Latin" title="Help:IPA/Latin">[ɔktaːu̯iˈaːnʊs]</a></span>), the adjectival form of "Octavius". He is mainly known by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglicization" title="Anglicization">anglicization</a> "Octavian" (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">ok-<span style="font-size:90%">TAY</span>-vee-ən</i></a>) for the period between 44 and 27 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[8]</a></sup> Officially, he seems to have used simply "Gaius Caesar",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[9]</a></sup> and began styling himself <i><a href="/wiki/Divi_filius" title="Divi filius">divi filius</a></i> or <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">divi Iuli(i) filius</i></span> ("son of the divine Julius") after the deification of Caesar in 42 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESyme1958175,_179_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESyme1958175,_179-13">[10]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Imperator Caesar</b>. From 38 BC at the latest, Octavian officially dropped all of his names except "Caesar", and began using the victory title <i><a href="/wiki/Imperator" title="Imperator">imperator</a></i> ("commander") in place of the <a href="/wiki/Praenomen" title="Praenomen">traditional Roman forename</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESyme1958176,_179,_181–183,_185_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESyme1958176,_179,_181–183,_185-14">[11]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Imperator Caesar Augustus</b>: Following his 31 BC defeat of Mark Antony and <a href="/wiki/Cleopatra" title="Cleopatra">Cleopatra</a>, partly on his own insistence, on 16 January 27 BC the Roman Senate granted him the additional name "<a href="/wiki/Augustus_(title)" title="Augustus (title)">Augustus</a>" (<small>Latin: </small><span class="IPA" lang="la-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Latin" title="Help:IPA/Latin">[au̯ˈɡʊstʊs]</a></span>). Historians use this name to refer to him from 27 BC until his death in AD 14.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[12]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_life_of_Augustus" title="Early life of Augustus">Early life of Augustus</a></div>
<p>While his paternal family was from the <a href="/wiki/Volsci" title="Volsci">Volscian</a> town of <a href="/wiki/Velletri" title="Velletri">Velletri</a>, approximately 40 kilometres (25 mi) to the south-east of <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, Augustus was born in the city of Rome on 23 September 63 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsSuetonius12CaesarsAugustushtml5_5–6]_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsSuetonius12CaesarsAugustushtml5_5–6]-16">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[14]</a></sup> He was born at Ox Head, a small property on the <a href="/wiki/Palatine_Hill" title="Palatine Hill">Palatine Hill</a>, very close to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Forum" title="Roman Forum">Roman Forum</a>. He was given the name Gaius Octavius, and in his infancy he received the <a href="/wiki/Cognomen" title="Cognomen">cognomen</a> Thurinus, possibly commemorating his father's victory at <a href="/wiki/Thurii" title="Thurii">Thurii</a> over a rebellious band of <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_ancient_Rome" title="Slavery in ancient Rome">slaves</a> which occurred a few years after his birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsSuetonius12CaesarsAugustushtml7_7]_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsSuetonius12CaesarsAugustushtml7_7]-18">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Suetonius" title="Suetonius">Suetonius</a> wrote: "There are many indications that the Octavian family was in days of old a distinguished one at Velitrae; for not only was a street in the most frequented part of town long ago called Octavian, but an altar was shown there besides, consecrated by an Octavius. This man was leader in a war with a neighbouring town ..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_1_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_1-19">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to the crowded nature of Rome at the time, Octavius was taken to his father's home village at Velletri to be raised. Octavius mentions his father's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equestrian_(Roman)" title="Equestrian (Roman)">equestrian</a> family only briefly in his memoirs. His paternal great-grandfather <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Octavius_(tribune_216_BC)" title="Gaius Octavius (tribune 216 BC)">Gaius Octavius</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Military_tribune" title="Military tribune">military tribune</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Punic_War" title="Second Punic War">Second Punic War</a>. His grandfather had served in several local political offices. His father, also named <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Octavius_(father_of_Augustus)" title="Gaius Octavius (father of Augustus)">Gaius Octavius</a>, had been <a href="/wiki/Roman_governor" title="Roman governor">governor</a> of <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(Roman_province)" title="Macedonia (Roman province)">Macedonia</a>. His mother, <a href="/wiki/Atia_(mother_of_Augustus)" title="Atia (mother of Augustus)">Atia</a>, was the niece of Julius Caesar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsSuetonius12CaesarsAugustushtml1_1–4]_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsSuetonius12CaesarsAugustushtml1_1–4]-20">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196214_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196214-21">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RSC_0022_-_transparent_background.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="171" data-file-width="332" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/RSC_0022_-_transparent_background.png/300px-RSC_0022_-_transparent_background.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e2/RSC_0022_-_transparent_background.png 1.5x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RSC_0022_-_transparent_background.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarius</a> from 44 BC, showing <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> on the obverse and the goddess <a href="/wiki/Venus_(mythology)" title="Venus (mythology)">Venus</a> on the reverse of the coin. Caption: CAESAR IMP. M. / L. AEMILIVS BVCA</div></div></div>
<p>In 59 BC, when he was four years old, his father died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholmFerguson198123_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholmFerguson198123-22">[19]</a></sup> His mother married a former governor of Syria, <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Marcius_Philippus_(consul_56_BC)" title="Lucius Marcius Philippus (consul 56 BC)">Lucius Marcius Philippus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsSuetonius12CaesarsAugustushtml4_4–8]_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsSuetonius12CaesarsAugustushtml4_4–8]-23">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicolaus_of_Damascus3_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicolaus_of_Damascus3-24">[21]</a></sup> Philippus claimed descent from <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, and was elected <a href="/wiki/Roman_consul" title="Roman consul">consul</a> in 56 BC. Philippus never had much of an interest in young Octavius. Because of this, Octavius was raised by his grandmother, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julia_Minor_(sister_of_Caesar)" title="Julia Minor (sister of Caesar)">Julia</a>, the sister of Julius Caesar. Julia died in 52 or 51 BC, and Octavius delivered the funeral oration for his grandmother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsSuetonius12CaesarsAugustushtml8_8.1]_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsSuetonius12CaesarsAugustushtml8_8.1]-25">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>From this point, his mother and stepfather took a more active role in raising him. He donned the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toga_virilis" title="Toga virilis">toga virilis</a></i> four years later,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suet8.1_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Suet8.1-28">[25]</a></sup> and was elected to the <a href="/wiki/College_of_Pontiffs" title="College of Pontiffs">College of Pontiffs</a> in 47 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicolaus_of_Damascus[httpwwwcsunedu~hcfll004nicolaushtml_4]_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicolaus_of_Damascus[httpwwwcsunedu~hcfll004nicolaushtml_4]-29">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196216_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196216-30">[27]</a></sup> The following year he was put in charge of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Olympic_Games" title="Ancient Olympic Games">Greek games</a> that were staged in honor of the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Venus_Genetrix" title="Temple of Venus Genetrix">Temple of Venus Genetrix</a>, built by Julius Caesar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196216_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196216-30">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_of_Damascus" title="Nicolaus of Damascus">Nicolaus of Damascus</a>, Octavius wished to join Caesar's staff for his campaign in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Africa_(province)" title="Africa (province)">Africa</a>, but gave way when his mother protested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicolaus_of_Damascus[httpwwwcsunedu~hcfll004nicolaushtml_6]_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicolaus_of_Damascus[httpwwwcsunedu~hcfll004nicolaushtml_6]-31">[28]</a></sup> In 46 BC, she consented for him to join Caesar in <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a>, where he planned to fight the forces of <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a>, Caesar's late enemy, but Octavius fell ill and was unable to travel. When he had recovered, he sailed to the front, but was <a href="/wiki/Shipwreck" title="Shipwreck">shipwrecked</a>. After coming ashore with a handful of companions, he crossed hostile territory to Caesar's camp, which impressed his great-uncle considerably.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suet8.1_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Suet8.1-28">[25]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Velleius_Paterculus" title="Marcus Velleius Paterculus">Velleius Paterculus</a> reports that after that time, Caesar allowed the young man to share his carriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[29]</a></sup> When back in Rome, Caesar deposited a new will with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vestal_Virgins" title="Vestal Virgins">Vestal Virgins</a>, naming Octavius as the prime beneficiary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suetonius_Julius_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Suetonius_Julius-33">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_to_power">Rise to power</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heir_to_Caesar">Heir to Caesar</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vincenzo_Camuccini_-_La_morte_di_Cesare.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1161" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Vincenzo_Camuccini_-_La_morte_di_Cesare.jpg/220px-Vincenzo_Camuccini_-_La_morte_di_Cesare.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Vincenzo_Camuccini_-_La_morte_di_Cesare.jpg/330px-Vincenzo_Camuccini_-_La_morte_di_Cesare.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Vincenzo_Camuccini_-_La_morte_di_Cesare.jpg/440px-Vincenzo_Camuccini_-_La_morte_di_Cesare.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vincenzo_Camuccini_-_La_morte_di_Cesare.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Death of Caesar</i> by <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Camuccini" title="Vincenzo Camuccini">Vincenzo Camuccini</a>. On 15 March 44 BC, Octavius's adoptive father Julius Caesar was assassinated by a conspiracy led by <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Junius_Brutus" title="Marcus Junius Brutus">Marcus Junius Brutus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Cassius_Longinus" title="Gaius Cassius Longinus">Gaius Cassius Longinus</a>. <a href="/wiki/Galleria_Nazionale_d%27Arte_Moderna" title="Galleria Nazionale d'Arte Moderna">Galleria Nazionale d'Arte Moderna</a>, Rome</div></div></div>
<p>Octavius was studying and undergoing military training in <a href="/wiki/Apollonia_(Illyria)" title="Apollonia (Illyria)">Apollonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illyria" title="Illyria">Illyria</a>, when <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Julius_Caesar" title="Assassination of Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar was assassinated</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Ides_of_March" title="Ides of March">Ides of March</a> (15 March) 44 BC. He rejected the advice of some army officers to take refuge with the troops in Macedonia and sailed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italia_(Roman_province)" title="Italia (Roman province)">Italy</a> to ascertain whether he had any potential political fortunes or security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20039_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20039-34">[31]</a></sup> Caesar had no living legitimate children under Roman law,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[d]</a></sup> and so had <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adoption_in_Rome" title="Adoption in Rome">adopted</a> Octavius, his grand-nephew, making him his primary heir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196215_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196215-36">[32]</a></sup> Mark Antony later charged that Octavian had earned his adoption by Caesar through sexual favours, though <a href="/wiki/Suetonius" title="Suetonius">Suetonius</a> describes Antony's accusation as <a href="/wiki/Defamation" title="Defamation">political slander</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[33]</a></sup> This form of slander was popular during this time in the Roman Republic to demean and discredit political opponents by accusing them of having an inappropriate sexual affair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[35]</a></sup> After landing at Lupiae near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brundisium" title="Brundisium">Brundisium</a>, Octavius learned the contents of Caesar's will, and only then did he decide to become Caesar's political heir as well as heir to two-thirds of his estate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196214_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196214-21">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20039_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20039-34">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Upon his adoption, Octavius assumed his great-uncle's name Gaius Julius Caesar. Roman citizens adopted into a new family usually retained their old <a href="/wiki/Nomen_gentilicium" title="Nomen gentilicium">nomen</a> in cognomen form (e.g., <i>Octavianus</i> for one who had been an Octavius, <i>Aemilianus</i> for one who had been an Aemilius, etc.). However, though some of his contemporaries did,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[37]</a></sup> there is no evidence that Octavius ever himself officially used the name <i>Octavianus</i>, as it would have made his modest origins too obvious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMackay2004160_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMackay2004160-42">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200310_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200310-43">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESouthern199820–21_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESouthern199820–21-44">[40]</a></sup> Historians usually refer to the new Caesar as <i>Octavian</i> during the time between his adoption and his assumption of the name Augustus in 27 BC in order to avoid confusing the dead dictator with his heir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESouthern199821_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESouthern199821-45">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Octavian could not rely on his limited funds to make a successful entry into the upper echelons of the Roman political hierarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20039–10_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20039–10-46">[42]</a></sup> After a warm welcome by Caesar's soldiers at Brundisium,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196219_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196219-47">[43]</a></sup> Octavian demanded a portion of the funds that were allotted by Caesar for the intended war against the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a> in the Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20039–10_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20039–10-46">[42]</a></sup> This amounted to 700 million <a href="/wiki/Sestertius" title="Sestertius">sesterces</a> stored at Brundisium, the staging ground in Italy for military operations in the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196218_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196218-48">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>A later senatorial investigation into the disappearance of the public funds took no action against Octavian, since he subsequently used that money to raise troops against the Senate's arch enemy Mark Antony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196219_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196219-47">[43]</a></sup> Octavian made another bold move in 44 BC when, without official permission, he appropriated the annual tribute that had been sent from Rome's <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near Eastern</a> province to Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200310_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200310-43">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200518_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200518-49">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Octavian began to bolster his personal forces with Caesar's veteran <a href="/wiki/Legionary" title="Legionary">legionaries</a> and with troops designated for the Parthian war, gathering support by emphasizing his status as heir to Caesar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20039_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20039-34">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[46]</a></sup> On his march to Rome through Italy, Octavian's presence and newly acquired funds attracted many, winning over Caesar's former veterans stationed in <a href="/wiki/Campania" title="Campania">Campania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200310_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200310-43">[39]</a></sup> By June, he had gathered an army of 3,000 loyal veterans, paying each a salary of 500 <a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarii</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholmFerguson198124,_27_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholmFerguson198124,_27-51">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196220_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196220-52">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Growing_tensions">Growing tensions</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Octave_(13668015683).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1801" data-file-width="1313" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Octave_%2813668015683%29.jpg/170px-Octave_%2813668015683%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Octave_%2813668015683%29.jpg/255px-Octave_%2813668015683%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Octave_%2813668015683%29.jpg/340px-Octave_%2813668015683%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Octave_(13668015683).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A bust of Augustus as a younger Octavian, dated ca. 30 BC. <a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Museums" title="Capitoline Museums">Capitoline Museums</a>, Rome</div></div></div>
<p>Arriving in Rome on 6 May 44 BC, Octavian found consul <a href="/wiki/Mark_Antony" title="Mark Antony">Mark Antony</a>, Caesar's former colleague, in an uneasy truce with the dictator's assassins. They had been granted a general amnesty on 17 March, yet Antony had succeeded in driving most of them out of Rome with an inflammatory <a href="/wiki/Eulogy" title="Eulogy">eulogy</a> at Caesar's funeral, mounting public opinion against the assassins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200310_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200310-43">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mark Antony was amassing political support, but Octavian still had opportunity to rival him as the leading member of the faction supporting Caesar. Mark Antony had lost the support of many Romans and supporters of Caesar when he initially opposed the motion to elevate Caesar to divine status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200311_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200311-53">[49]</a></sup> Octavian failed to persuade Antony to relinquish Caesar's money to him. During the summer, he managed to win support from Caesarian sympathizers and also made common with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optimates" title="Optimates">Optimates</a>, the former enemies of Caesar, who saw him as the lesser evil and hoped to manipulate him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESyme1939114–120_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESyme1939114–120-54">[50]</a></sup> In September, the leading Optimate orator <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Marcus Tullius Cicero</a> began to attack Antony in a <a href="/wiki/Philippicae" title="Philippicae">series of speeches</a> portraying him as a threat to the Republican order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholmFerguson198126_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholmFerguson198126-55">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196230_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196230-56">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_conflict_with_Antony">First conflict with Antony</span></h3>
<p>With opinion in Rome turning against him and his year of consular power nearing its end, Antony attempted to pass laws that would assign him the province of <a href="/wiki/Cisalpine_Gaul" title="Cisalpine Gaul">Cisalpine Gaul</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200311–12_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200311–12-57">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196221_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196221-58">[54]</a></sup> Octavian meanwhile built up a private army in Italy by recruiting Caesarian veterans and, on 28 November, he won over two of Antony's legions with the enticing offer of monetary gain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESyme1939123–126_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESyme1939123–126-59">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200312_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200312-60">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196223_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196223-61">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the face of Octavian's large and capable force, Antony saw the danger of staying in Rome and, to the relief of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_senate" title="Roman senate">Senate</a>, he left Rome for Cisalpine Gaul, which was to be handed to him on 1 January.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196223_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196223-61">[57]</a></sup> However, the province had earlier been assigned to <a href="/wiki/Decimus_Junius_Brutus_Albinus" title="Decimus Junius Brutus Albinus">Decimus Junius Brutus Albinus</a>, one of Caesar's assassins, who now refused to yield to Antony. Antony besieged him at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutina" title="Mutina">Mutina</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196224_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196224-62">[58]</a></sup> and rejected the resolutions passed by the Senate to stop the fighting. The Senate had no army to enforce their resolutions. This provided an opportunity for Octavian, who already was known to have armed forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200312_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200312-60">[56]</a></sup> Cicero also defended Octavian against Antony's taunts about Octavian's lack of noble lineage and aping of Julius Caesar's name, stating "we have no more brilliant example of traditional piety among our youth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholmFerguson198129_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholmFerguson198129-63">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the urging of Cicero, the Senate inducted Octavian as senator on 1 January 43 BC, yet he also was given the power to vote alongside the former consuls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200313_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200313-64">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196223_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196223-61">[57]</a></sup> In addition, Octavian was granted <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propraetor" title="Propraetor">propraetor</a></i> <i><a href="/wiki/Imperium" title="Imperium">imperium</a></i> (commanding power) which legalized his command of troops, sending him to relieve the siege along with <a href="/wiki/Aulus_Hirtius" title="Aulus Hirtius">Hirtius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Vibius_Pansa_Caetronianus" title="Gaius Vibius Pansa Caetronianus">Pansa</a> (the consuls for 43 BC). <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200313_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200313-64">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESyme1939167_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESyme1939167-65">[61]</a></sup> He assumed the <i><a href="/wiki/Fasces" title="Fasces">fasces</a></i> on 7 January,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFishwick2004250_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFishwick2004250-66">[62]</a></sup> a date that he would later commemorate as the beginning of his public career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196224_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196224-62">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200313_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200313-64">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen2005160_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen2005160-67">[63]</a></sup> Antony's forces were defeated at the battles of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Forum_Gallorum" title="Battle of Forum Gallorum">Forum Gallorum</a> (14 April) and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mutina" title="Battle of Mutina">Mutina</a> (21 April), forcing Antony to retreat to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transalpine_Gaul" title="Transalpine Gaul">Transalpine Gaul</a>. Both consuls were killed, however, leaving Octavian in sole command of their armies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESyme1939173–174_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESyme1939173–174-68">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScullard1982157_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScullard1982157-69">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>The senate heaped many more rewards on Decimus Brutus than on Octavian for defeating Antony, then attempted to give command of the consular legions to Decimus Brutus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196226–27_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196226–27-70">[66]</a></sup> In response, Octavian stayed in the <a href="/wiki/Po_Valley" title="Po Valley">Po Valley</a> and refused to aid any further offensive against Antony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196227_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196227-71">[67]</a></sup> In July, an embassy of <a href="/wiki/Centurion" title="Centurion">centurions</a> sent by Octavian entered Rome and demanded the consulship left vacant by Hirtius and Pansa<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholmFerguson198132–33_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholmFerguson198132–33-72">[68]</a></sup> and also that the decree should be rescinded which declared Antony a public enemy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196227_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196227-71">[67]</a></sup> When this was refused, he marched on the city with eight legions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196227_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196227-71">[67]</a></sup> He encountered no military opposition in Rome, and on 19 August 43 BC was elected consul with his relative <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Pedius_(consul)" title="Quintus Pedius (consul)">Quintus Pedius</a> as co-consul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200315_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200315-73">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196228_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196228-74">[70]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Antony formed an alliance with <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aemilius_Lepidus_(triumvir)" title="Marcus Aemilius Lepidus (triumvir)">Marcus Aemilius Lepidus</a>, another leading Caesarian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESyme1939176–186_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESyme1939176–186-75">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_Triumvirate">Second Triumvirate</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Proscriptions">Proscriptions</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antony_with_Octavian_aureus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="194" data-file-width="386" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Antony_with_Octavian_aureus.jpg/300px-Antony_with_Octavian_aureus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8b/Antony_with_Octavian_aureus.jpg 1.5x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antony_with_Octavian_aureus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roman <a href="/wiki/Aureus" title="Aureus">aureus</a> bearing the portraits of <a href="/wiki/Mark_Antony" title="Mark Antony">Mark Antony</a> (left) and Octavian (right), issued in 41 BC to celebrate the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Triumvirate" title="Second Triumvirate">Second Triumvirate</a> by Octavian, Antony and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aemilius_Lepidus_(triumvir)" title="Marcus Aemilius Lepidus (triumvir)">Marcus Lepidus</a> in 43 BC. Both sides bear the inscription "III VIR R P C", meaning "One of Three Men for the regulation of the Republic". Caption: M. ANT. IMP. AVG. III VIR RPC M. BARBAT. Q. P. / CAESAR IMP. PONT. III VIR PRC. The M. Barbatius Pollio was a moneyer<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[72]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In a meeting near <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a> in October 43 BC, Octavian, Antony, and Lepidus formed the <a href="/wiki/Second_Triumvirate" title="Second Triumvirate">Second Triumvirate</a>. Their powers were officialized by the Senate on 27 November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200316_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200316-77">[73]</a></sup> This explicit arrogation of special powers lasting five years was then legalised by law passed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plebs" title="Plebs">plebs</a>, unlike the unofficial <a href="/wiki/First_Triumvirate" title="First Triumvirate">First Triumvirate</a> formed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Pompeius_Magnus" title="Gnaeus Pompeius Magnus">Pompey</a>, Julius Caesar, and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Licinius_Crassus" title="Marcus Licinius Crassus">Marcus Licinius Crassus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200315_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200315-73">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScullard1982163_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScullard1982163-78">[74]</a></sup> The triumvirs then set in motion <a href="/wiki/Proscription" title="Proscription">proscriptions</a>, in which between 130 and 300 senators<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[e]</a></sup> and 2,000 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equestrian_(Roman)" title="Equestrian (Roman)">equites</a></i> were branded as <a href="/wiki/Outlaw" title="Outlaw">outlaws</a> and deprived of their property and, for those who failed to escape, their lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200316_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200316-77">[73]</a></sup> This decree issued by the triumvirate was motivated in part by a need to raise money to pay the salaries of their troops for the upcoming conflict against Caesar's assassins, <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Junius_Brutus" title="Marcus Junius Brutus">Marcus Junius Brutus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Cassius_Longinus" title="Gaius Cassius Longinus">Gaius Cassius Longinus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScullard1982164_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScullard1982164-81">[76]</a></sup> Rewards for their arrest gave incentive for Romans to capture those proscribed, while the assets and properties of those arrested were seized by the triumvirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200316_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200316-77">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Contemporary Roman historians provide conflicting reports as to which triumvir was most responsible for the proscriptions and killing.  However, the sources agree that enacting the proscriptions was a means by all three factions to eliminate political enemies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott193319–20_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott193319–20-82">[77]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Velleius_Paterculus" title="Marcus Velleius Paterculus">Marcus Velleius Paterculus</a> asserted that Octavian tried to avoid proscribing officials whereas Lepidus and Antony were to blame for initiating them. <a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">Cassius Dio</a> defended Octavian as trying to spare as many as possible, whereas Antony and Lepidus, being older and involved in politics longer, had many more enemies to deal with.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott193319_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott193319-83">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>This claim was rejected by Appian, who maintained that Octavian shared an equal interest with Lepidus and Antony in eradicating his enemies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott193320_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott193320-84">[79]</a></sup> Suetonius said that Octavian was reluctant to proscribe officials, but did pursue his enemies with more vigor than the other triumvirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott193319–20_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott193319–20-82">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> described the proscriptions as a ruthless and cutthroat swapping of friends and family among Antony, Lepidus, and Octavian. For example, Octavian allowed the proscription of his ally Cicero, Antony the proscription of his maternal uncle <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Julius_Caesar_(consul_64_BC)" title="Lucius Julius Caesar (consul 64 BC)">Lucius Julius Caesar</a> (the consul of 64 BC), and Lepidus his brother <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucius_Aemilius_Lepidus_Paullus" title="Lucius Aemilius Lepidus Paullus">Paullus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott193319_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott193319-83">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Philippi_and_division_of_territory">Battle of Philippi and division of territory</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Liberators%27_civil_war" title="Liberators' civil war">Liberators' civil war</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:S0484.4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="294" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/S0484.4.jpg/300px-S0484.4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/S0484.4.jpg/450px-S0484.4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/S0484.4.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:S0484.4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarius</a> minted <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 18 BC. Obverse: CAESAR AVGVSTVS; reverse: comet of eight rays with tail upward; DIVVS IVLIV[S] (DIVINE JULIUS).</div></div></div>
<p>On 1 January 42 BC, the Senate posthumously recognized Julius Caesar as a divinity of the Roman state, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divus_Iulius" title="Divus Iulius">Divus Iulius</a></i>. Octavian was able to further his cause by emphasizing the fact that he was <i><a href="/wiki/Divi_filius" title="Divi filius">divi filius</a></i>, "Son of the Divine".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESyme1939202_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESyme1939202-85">[80]</a></sup> Antony and Octavian then sent 28 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_legions" title="Roman legions">legions</a> by sea to face the armies of Brutus and Cassius, who had built their base of power in Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200317_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200317-86">[81]</a></sup> After two <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Philippi" title="Battle of Philippi">battles at Philippi</a> in Macedonia in October 42, the Caesarian army was victorious and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Junius_Brutus" title="Marcus Junius Brutus">Brutus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Cassius_Longinus" title="Gaius Cassius Longinus">Cassius</a> committed <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a>. Mark Antony later used the examples of these battles as a means to belittle Octavian, as both battles were decisively won with the use of Antony's forces. In addition to claiming responsibility for both victories, Antony also branded Octavian as a coward for handing over his direct military control to <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Vipsanius_Agrippa" title="Marcus Vipsanius Agrippa">Marcus Vipsanius Agrippa</a> instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200317–18_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200317–18-87">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>After Philippi, a new territorial arrangement was made among the members of the Second Triumvirate. <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a> and the province of Hispania were placed in the hands of Octavian. Antony traveled east to Egypt where he allied himself with Queen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleopatra_VII_of_Egypt" title="Cleopatra VII of Egypt">Cleopatra VII</a>, the former lover of Julius Caesar and mother of Caesar's infant son <a href="/wiki/Caesarion" title="Caesarion">Caesarion</a>. Lepidus was left with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Africa_Province" title="Africa Province">province of Africa</a>, stymied by Antony, who conceded Hispania to Octavian instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200318_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200318-88">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Octavian was left to decide where in Italy to settle the tens of thousands of veterans of the Macedonian campaign, whom the triumvirs had promised to discharge. The tens of thousands who had fought on the republican side with Brutus and Cassius could easily ally with a political opponent of Octavian if not appeased, and they also required land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200318_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200318-88">[83]</a></sup> There was no more government-controlled land to allot as settlements for their soldiers, so Octavian had to choose one of two options: alienating many Roman citizens by confiscating their land, or alienating many Roman soldiers who could mount a considerable opposition against him in the Roman heartland. Octavian chose the former.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200318–19_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200318–19-89">[84]</a></sup> There were as many as eighteen Roman towns affected by the new settlements, with entire populations driven out or at least given partial evictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200319_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200319-90">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rebellion_and_marriage_alliances">Rebellion and marriage alliances</span></h4>
<p>There was widespread dissatisfaction with Octavian over these settlements of his soldiers, and this encouraged many to rally at the side of <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Antonius_(brother_of_Mark_Antony)" title="Lucius Antonius (brother of Mark Antony)">Lucius Antonius</a>, who was brother of Mark Antony and supported by a majority in the Senate. Meanwhile, Octavian asked for a divorce from <a href="/wiki/Claudia_(wife_of_Octavian)" title="Claudia (wife of Octavian)">Claudia</a>, the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Fulvia" title="Fulvia">Fulvia</a> (Mark Antony's wife) and her first husband <a href="/wiki/Publius_Clodius_Pulcher" title="Publius Clodius Pulcher">Publius Clodius Pulcher</a>. He returned Claudia to her mother, claiming that their marriage had never been consummated. Fulvia decided to take action. Together with Lucius Antonius, she raised an army in Italy to fight for Antony's rights against Octavian. Lucius and Fulvia took a political and martial gamble in opposing Octavian, however, since the Roman army still depended on the triumvirs for their salaries. Lucius and his allies ended up in a defensive siege at <a href="/wiki/Perusia" title="Perusia">Perusia</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Perugia" title="Perugia">Perugia</a>), where Octavian <a href="/wiki/Perusine_War" title="Perusine War">forced them into surrender</a> in early 40 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200319_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200319-90">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Domus-augusti-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="3970" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Domus-augusti-2.jpg/350px-Domus-augusti-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Domus-augusti-2.jpg/525px-Domus-augusti-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Domus-augusti-2.jpg/700px-Domus-augusti-2.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Domus-augusti-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fresco paintings inside the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Augustus" title="House of Augustus">House of Augustus</a>, his residence during his reign as emperor.</div></div></div>
<p>Lucius and his army were spared, due to his kinship with Antony, the strongman of the East, while Fulvia was exiled to <a href="/wiki/Sicyon" title="Sicyon">Sicyon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196232_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196232-91">[86]</a></sup> Octavian showed no mercy, however, for the mass of allies loyal to Lucius; on 15 March, the anniversary of Julius Caesar's assassination, he had 300 Roman senators and equestrians executed for allying with Lucius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200320_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200320-92">[87]</a></sup> Perusia also was pillaged and burned as a warning for others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196232_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196232-91">[86]</a></sup> This bloody event sullied Octavian's reputation and was criticized by many, such as Augustan poet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sextus_Propertius" title="Sextus Propertius">Sextus Propertius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200320_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200320-92">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sextus_Pompeius" title="Sextus Pompeius">Sextus Pompeius</a>, the son of Pompey and still a renegade general following Julius Caesar's victory over his father, had established himself in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a> as part of an agreement reached with the Second Triumvirate in 39 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScullard1982162_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScullard1982162-93">[88]</a></sup> Both Antony and Octavian were vying for an alliance with Pompeius. Octavian succeeded in a temporary alliance in 40 BC when he married <a href="/wiki/Scribonia_(wife_of_Octavian)" title="Scribonia (wife of Octavian)">Scribonia</a>, a sister or daughter of Pompeius's father-in-law <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Scribonius_Libo_(consul_34_BC)" title="Lucius Scribonius Libo (consul 34 BC)">Lucius Scribonius Libo</a>. Scribonia gave birth to Octavian's only natural child, <a href="/wiki/Julia_the_Elder" title="Julia the Elder">Julia</a>, the same day that he divorced her to marry <a href="/wiki/Livia" title="Livia">Livia Drusilla</a>, little more than a year after their marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200320_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200320-92">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>While in Egypt, Antony had been engaged in an affair with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleopatra_VII" title="Cleopatra VII">Cleopatra</a> and had fathered twin children with her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[f]</a></sup> Aware of his deteriorating relationship with Octavian, Antony left Cleopatra; he sailed to Italy in 40 BC with a large force to oppose Octavian, laying siege to Brundisium. This new conflict proved untenable for both Octavian and Antony, however. Their centurions, who had become important figures politically, refused to fight due to their Caesarian cause, while the legions under their command followed suit. Meanwhile, in Sicyon, Antony's wife Fulvia died of a sudden illness while Antony was <i>en route</i> to meet her. Fulvia's death and the mutiny of their centurions allowed the two remaining triumvirs to effect a reconciliation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200321_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200321-95">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200519_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200519-96">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the autumn of 40, Octavian and Antony approved the Treaty of Brundisium, by which Lepidus would remain in Africa, Antony in the East, Octavian in the West. The <a href="/wiki/Italian_Peninsula" title="Italian Peninsula">Italian Peninsula</a> was left open to all for the recruitment of soldiers, but in reality, this provision was useless for Antony in the East. To further cement relations of alliance with Mark Antony, Octavian gave his sister, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Octavia_Minor" title="Octavia Minor">Octavia Minor</a>, in marriage to Antony in late 40 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200321_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200321-95">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="War_with_Pompeius">War with Pompeius</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_between_Sextus_Pompey_and_the_Second_Triumvirate" title="War between Sextus Pompey and the Second Triumvirate">War between Sextus Pompey and the Second Triumvirate</a></div>
<p>Sextus Pompeius threatened Octavian in Italy by denying shipments of grain through the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> to the peninsula. Pompeius's own son was put in charge as naval commander in the effort to cause widespread famine in Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200519_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200519-96">[90]</a></sup> Pompeius's control over the sea prompted him to take on the name <i>Neptuni filius</i>, "son of <a href="/wiki/Neptune_(mythology)" title="Neptune (mythology)">Neptune</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200322_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200322-97">[91]</a></sup> A temporary peace agreement was reached in 39 BC with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Misenum" title="Treaty of Misenum">treaty of Misenum</a>; the blockade on Italy was lifted once Octavian granted Pompeius Sardinia, <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsica</a>, Sicily, and the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a>, and ensured him a future position as consul for 35 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200519_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200519-96">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200322_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200322-97">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>The territorial agreement between the triumvirate and Sextus Pompeius began to crumble once Octavian divorced Scribonia and married Livia on 17 January 38 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200323_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200323-98">[92]</a></sup> One of Pompeius's naval commanders betrayed him and handed over Corsica and Sardinia to Octavian. Octavian lacked the resources to confront Pompeius alone, however, so an agreement was reached with the Second Triumvirate's extension for another five-year period beginning in 37 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScullard1982163_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScullard1982163-78">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200324_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200324-99">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Denarius_Sextus_Pompeius-Scilla.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="186" data-file-width="379" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Denarius_Sextus_Pompeius-Scilla.jpg/300px-Denarius_Sextus_Pompeius-Scilla.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/19/Denarius_Sextus_Pompeius-Scilla.jpg 1.5x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Denarius_Sextus_Pompeius-Scilla.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarius</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sextus_Pompeius" title="Sextus Pompeius">Sextus Pompeius</a>, minted for his victory over Octavian's fleet. Obverse: the place where he defeated Octavian, Pharus of <a href="/wiki/Messina" title="Messina">Messina</a> decorated with a statue of Neptune; before that galley adorned with aquila, sceptre &amp; trident; MAG. PIVS IMP. ITER. Reverse, the monster <a href="/wiki/Scylla" title="Scylla">Scylla</a>, her torso of dogs and fish tails, wielding a rudder as a club. Caption: PRAEF[ECTUS] CLAS[SIS] ET ORAE MARIT[IMAE] EX S. C.</div></div></div>
<p>In supporting Octavian, Antony expected to gain support for his own campaign against the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a>, desiring to avenge Rome's <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carrhae" title="Battle of Carrhae">defeat at Carrhae</a> in 53 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200324_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200324-99">[93]</a></sup> In an agreement reached at <a href="/wiki/Taranto" title="Taranto">Tarentum</a>, Antony provided 120 ships for Octavian to use against Pompeius, while Octavian was to send 20,000 <a href="/wiki/Legionary" title="Legionary">legionaries</a> to Antony for use against Parthia. Octavian sent only a tenth of those promised, however, which Antony viewed as an intentional provocation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200325_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200325-100">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Octavian and Lepidus launched a joint operation against Sextus in Sicily in 36 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200325–26_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200325–26-101">[95]</a></sup> Despite setbacks for Octavian, the naval fleet of Sextus Pompeius was almost entirely destroyed on 3 September by General Agrippa at the naval <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Naulochus" title="Battle of Naulochus">Battle of Naulochus</a>. Sextus fled to the east with his remaining forces, where he was captured and executed in <a href="/wiki/Miletus" title="Miletus">Miletus</a> by one of Antony's generals the following year. As Lepidus and Octavian accepted the surrender of Pompeius's troops, Lepidus attempted to claim Sicily for himself, ordering Octavian to leave. Lepidus's troops deserted him, however, and defected to Octavian since they were weary of fighting and were enticed by Octavian's promises of money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200326_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200326-102">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Lepidus surrendered to Octavian and was permitted to retain the office of <i><a href="/wiki/Pontifex_maximus" title="Pontifex maximus">pontifex maximus</a></i> (head of the college of priests), but was ejected from the Triumvirate, his public career at an end, and effectively was exiled to a <a href="/wiki/Villa" title="Villa">villa</a> at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Circeo" title="Mount Circeo">Cape Circei</a> in Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScullard1982164_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScullard1982164-81">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200326_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200326-102">[96]</a></sup> The Roman dominions were now divided between Octavian in the West and Antony in the East. Octavian ensured Rome's citizens of their rights to property in order to maintain peace and stability in his portion of the Empire. This time, he settled his discharged soldiers outside of Italy, while also returning 30,000 slaves to their former Roman owners—slaves who had fled to join Pompeius's army and navy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200326–27_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200326–27-103">[97]</a></sup> Octavian had the Senate grant him, his wife, and his sister <a href="/wiki/Tribune_of_the_plebs" title="Tribune of the plebs">tribunal</a> <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_immunity" title="Sovereign immunity">immunity</a>, or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacrosanct" title="Sacrosanct">sacrosanctitas</a></i>, in order to ensure his own safety and that of Livia and Octavia once he returned to Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200327–28_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200327–28-104">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="War_with_Antony_and_Cleopatra">War with Antony and Cleopatra</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/War_of_Actium" title="War of Actium">War of Actium</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Donations_of_Alexandria" title="Donations of Alexandria">Donations of Alexandria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Cleopatra" title="Death of Cleopatra">Death of Cleopatra</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lawrence_Alma-Tadema-_Anthony_and_Cleopatra.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="892" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Lawrence_Alma-Tadema-_Anthony_and_Cleopatra.JPG/220px-Lawrence_Alma-Tadema-_Anthony_and_Cleopatra.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Lawrence_Alma-Tadema-_Anthony_and_Cleopatra.JPG/330px-Lawrence_Alma-Tadema-_Anthony_and_Cleopatra.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Lawrence_Alma-Tadema-_Anthony_and_Cleopatra.JPG/440px-Lawrence_Alma-Tadema-_Anthony_and_Cleopatra.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lawrence_Alma-Tadema-_Anthony_and_Cleopatra.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Anthony and Cleopatra</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Alma-Tadema" title="Lawrence Alma-Tadema">Lawrence Alma-Tadema</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Castro_Battle_of_Actium.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Castro_Battle_of_Actium.jpg/220px-Castro_Battle_of_Actium.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Castro_Battle_of_Actium.jpg/330px-Castro_Battle_of_Actium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Castro_Battle_of_Actium.jpg/440px-Castro_Battle_of_Actium.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Castro_Battle_of_Actium.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Actium" title="Battle of Actium">Battle of Actium</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/Laureys_a_Castro" title="Laureys a Castro">Laureys a Castro</a>, painted 1672, <a href="/wiki/National_Maritime_Museum" title="National Maritime Museum">National Maritime Museum</a>, London.</div></div></div>
<p>Meanwhile, Antony's campaign turned disastrous against Parthia, tarnishing his image as a leader, and the mere 2,000 legionaries sent by Octavian to Antony were hardly enough to replenish his forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200329_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200329-105">[99]</a></sup> On the other hand, Cleopatra could restore his army to full strength; he already was engaged in a romantic affair with her, so he decided to send Octavia back to Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200329–30_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200329–30-106">[100]</a></sup> Octavian used this to spread <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a> implying that Antony was becoming less than Roman because he rejected a legitimate Roman spouse for an "Oriental <a href="/wiki/Intimate_relationship" title="Intimate relationship">paramour</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200330_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200330-107">[101]</a></sup> In 36 BC, Octavian used a political ploy to make himself look less autocratic and Antony more the villain by proclaiming that the civil wars were coming to an end, and that he would step down as triumvir—if only Antony would do the same. Antony refused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200520_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200520-108">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>Roman troops captured the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Armenia_(antiquity)" title="Kingdom of Armenia (antiquity)">Kingdom of Armenia</a> in 34 BC, and Antony made his son <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Helios" title="Alexander Helios">Alexander Helios</a> the ruler of Armenia. He also awarded the title "<a href="/wiki/King_of_Kings" title="King of Kings">Queen of Kings</a>" to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reign_of_Cleopatra_VII" title="Reign of Cleopatra VII">Cleopatra</a>, acts that Octavian used to convince the Roman Senate that Antony had ambitions to diminish the preeminence of Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200330_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200330-107">[101]</a></sup> Octavian became consul once again on 1 January 33 BC, and he opened the following session in the Senate with a vehement attack on Antony's <a href="/wiki/Donations_of_Alexandria" title="Donations of Alexandria">grants of titles and territories</a> to his relatives and to his queen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200331_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200331-109">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>The breach between Antony and Octavian prompted a large portion of the Senators, as well as both of that year's consuls, to leave Rome and defect to Antony. However, Octavian received two key deserters from Antony in the autumn of 32 BC: Munatius Plancus and Marcus Titius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200332–34_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200332–34-110">[104]</a></sup> These defectors gave Octavian the information that he needed to confirm with the Senate all the accusations that he made against Antony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200334_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200334-111">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Octavian forcibly entered the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Vesta" title="Temple of Vesta">temple of the Vestal Virgins</a> and seized Antony's secret will, which he promptly publicized. The will would have given away Roman-conquered territories as kingdoms for his sons to rule, and designated <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> as the site for a tomb for him and his queen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200334–35_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200334–35-112">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200521–22_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200521–22-113">[107]</a></sup> In late 32 BC, the Senate officially revoked Antony's powers as consul and declared war on Cleopatra's regime in Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200335_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200335-114">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200522_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200522-115">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus_and_Cupid_from_the_House_of_Marcus_Fabius_Rufus_at_Pompeii,_most_likely_a_depiction_of_Cleopatra_VII.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="472" data-file-width="662" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Venus_and_Cupid_from_the_House_of_Marcus_Fabius_Rufus_at_Pompeii%2C_most_likely_a_depiction_of_Cleopatra_VII.jpg/220px-Venus_and_Cupid_from_the_House_of_Marcus_Fabius_Rufus_at_Pompeii%2C_most_likely_a_depiction_of_Cleopatra_VII.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Venus_and_Cupid_from_the_House_of_Marcus_Fabius_Rufus_at_Pompeii%2C_most_likely_a_depiction_of_Cleopatra_VII.jpg/330px-Venus_and_Cupid_from_the_House_of_Marcus_Fabius_Rufus_at_Pompeii%2C_most_likely_a_depiction_of_Cleopatra_VII.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Venus_and_Cupid_from_the_House_of_Marcus_Fabius_Rufus_at_Pompeii%2C_most_likely_a_depiction_of_Cleopatra_VII.jpg/440px-Venus_and_Cupid_from_the_House_of_Marcus_Fabius_Rufus_at_Pompeii%2C_most_likely_a_depiction_of_Cleopatra_VII.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venus_and_Cupid_from_the_House_of_Marcus_Fabius_Rufus_at_Pompeii,_most_likely_a_depiction_of_Cleopatra_VII.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This mid-1st-century-BC <a href="/wiki/Pompeian_Styles" title="Pompeian Styles">Roman wall painting</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a>, Italy, showing <a href="/wiki/Venus_(mythology)" title="Venus (mythology)">Venus</a> holding a <a href="/wiki/Cupid" title="Cupid">cupid</a> is most likely a depiction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleopatra_VII" title="Cleopatra VII">Cleopatra VII</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Egypt" title="Ptolemaic Egypt">Ptolemaic Egypt</a> as <a href="/wiki/Venus_Genetrix_(sculpture)" title="Venus Genetrix (sculpture)">Venus Genetrix</a>, with her son <a href="/wiki/Caesarion" title="Caesarion">Caesarion</a> as the cupid, similar in appearance to the now lost statue of Cleopatra erected by <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Venus_Genetrix" title="Temple of Venus Genetrix">Temple of Venus Genetrix</a> (within the <a href="/wiki/Forum_of_Caesar" title="Forum of Caesar">Forum of Caesar</a>). The owner of the House of Marcus Fabius Rufus at Pompeii walled off the room with this painting, most likely in immediate reaction to the execution of Caesarion on orders of Augustus in 30 BC, when artistic depictions of Caesarion would have been considered <a href="/wiki/Damnatio_memoriae" title="Damnatio memoriae">a sensitive issue</a> for the ruling regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[111]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In early 31 BC, Antony and Cleopatra were temporarily stationed in Greece when Octavian gained a preliminary victory: the navy successfully ferried troops across the <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">Adriatic Sea</a> under the command of Agrippa. Agrippa cut off Antony and Cleopatra's main force from their supply routes at sea, while Octavian landed on the mainland opposite the island of Corcyra (modern <a href="/wiki/Corfu" title="Corfu">Corfu</a>) and marched south.
