Trapped on land and sea, deserters of Antony's army fled to Octavian's side daily while Octavian's forces were comfortable enough to make preparations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200337_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200337-118">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Antony's fleet sailed through the bay of <a href="/wiki/Actium" title="Actium">Actium</a> on the western coast of Greece in a desperate attempt to break free of the <a href="/wiki/Blockade" title="Blockade">naval blockade</a>. It was there that Antony's fleet faced the much larger fleet of smaller, more maneuverable ships under commanders Agrippa and <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Sosius" title="Gaius Sosius">Gaius Sosius</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Actium" title="Battle of Actium">Battle of Actium</a> on 2 September 31 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200338_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200338-119">[113]</a></sup> Antony and his remaining forces were spared only due to a last-ditch effort by Cleopatra's fleet that had been waiting nearby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200338–39_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200338–39-120">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Octavian_aureus_circa_30_BCE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1020" data-file-width="1061" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Octavian_aureus_circa_30_BCE.jpg/220px-Octavian_aureus_circa_30_BCE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Octavian_aureus_circa_30_BCE.jpg/330px-Octavian_aureus_circa_30_BCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Octavian_aureus_circa_30_BCE.jpg/440px-Octavian_aureus_circa_30_BCE.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Octavian_aureus_circa_30_BCE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aureus" title="Aureus">Aureus</a> of Octavian, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 30 BC, <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Octavian pursued them and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Alexandria_(30_BC)" title="Battle of Alexandria (30 BC)">defeated their forces</a> in Alexandria on 1 August 30 BC—after which <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Cleopatra" title="Death of Cleopatra">Antony and Cleopatra committed suicide</a>. Antony fell on his own sword and was taken by his soldiers back to Alexandria where he died in Cleopatra's arms. Cleopatra died soon after, reputedly by the venomous bite of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asp_(reptile)" title="Asp (reptile)">asp</a> or by poison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200339_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200339-121">[115]</a></sup> Octavian had exploited his position as Caesar's heir to further his own political career, and he was well aware of the dangers in allowing another person to do the same. He therefore followed the advice of <a href="/wiki/Arius_Didymus" title="Arius Didymus">Arius Didymus</a> that "two Caesars are one too many", ordering <a href="/wiki/Caesarion" title="Caesarion">Caesarion</a>, Julius Caesar's son by Cleopatra, killed, while sparing Cleopatra's children by Antony, with the exception of Antony's <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Antonius_Antyllus" title="Marcus Antonius Antyllus">older son</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen1990697_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen1990697-122">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScullard1982171_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScullard1982171-123">[117]</a></sup> Octavian had previously shown little mercy to surrendered enemies and acted in ways that had proven unpopular with the Roman people, yet he was given credit for pardoning many of his opponents after the Battle of Actium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200349_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200349-124">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sole_ruler_of_Rome">Sole ruler of Rome</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitutional_Reforms_of_Augustus" title="Constitutional Reforms of Augustus">Constitutional Reforms of Augustus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Propaganda_in_Augustan_Rome" title="Propaganda in Augustan Rome">Propaganda in Augustan Rome</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Coinage_reform_of_Augustus" title="Coinage reform of Augustus">Coinage reform of Augustus</a></div>
<p>After Actium and the defeat of Antony and Cleopatra, Octavian was in a position to rule the entire Republic under an unofficial <a href="/wiki/Principate" title="Principate">principate</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen200534–35_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen200534–35-125">[119]</a></sup>—but he had to achieve this through incremental power gains. He did so by courting the Senate and the people while upholding the republican traditions of Rome, appearing that he was not aspiring to dictatorship or monarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200524–25_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200524–25-126">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen200538–39_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen200538–39-127">[121]</a></sup> Marching into Rome, Octavian and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Vipsanius_Agrippa" title="Marcus Vipsanius Agrippa">Marcus Agrippa</a> were elected as <a href="/wiki/Roman_consul" title="Roman consul">consuls</a> by the Senate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200345_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200345-128">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>Years of civil war had left Rome in a state of near lawlessness, but the Republic was not prepared to accept the control of Octavian as a despot. At the same time, Octavian could not simply give up his authority without risking further civil wars among the Roman generals and, even if he desired no position of authority whatsoever, his position demanded that he look to the well-being of the city of Rome and the <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">Roman provinces</a>. Octavian's aims from this point forward were to return Rome to a state of stability, traditional legality, and civility by lifting the overt political pressure imposed on the courts of law and ensuring free elections—in name at least.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200344–45_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200344–45-129">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_settlement">First settlement</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Constitution of the Roman Empire">Constitution of the Roman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Constitution_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="History of the Constitution of the Roman Empire">History of the Constitution of the Roman Empire</a></div>
<p>On 13 January 27 BC, Octavian made a show of returning full power to the Roman Senate and relinquishing his control of the Roman provinces and their armies. Under his consulship, however, the Senate had little power in initiating legislation by introducing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bill_(proposed_law)" title="Bill (proposed law)">bills</a> for senatorial debate. Octavian was no longer in direct control of the provinces and their armies, but he retained the loyalty of active duty soldiers and veterans alike. The careers of many clients and adherents depended on his <a href="/wiki/Patronage" title="Patronage">patronage</a>, as his financial power was unrivaled in the Roman Republic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200345–50_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200345–50-130">[124]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Werner_Eck" title="Werner Eck">Werner Eck</a> states:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The sum of his power derived first of all from various powers of office delegated to him by the Senate and people, secondly from his immense private fortune, and thirdly from numerous patron-client relationships he established with individuals and groups throughout the Empire. All of them taken together formed the basis of his <i>auctoritas</i>, which he himself emphasized as the foundation of his political actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003113_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003113-131">[125]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>To a large extent, the public were aware of the vast financial resources that Octavian commanded. He failed to encourage enough senators to finance the building and maintenance of networks of roads in Italy in 20 BC, but he undertook direct responsibility for them. This was publicized on the Roman currency issued in 16 BC, after he donated vast amounts of money to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerarium_Saturni" title="Aerarium Saturni">aerarium Saturni</a></i>, the public treasury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200380_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200380-132">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Statue_de_L%27Empereur_Auguste_-_MR_99_-_Ma_1278.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4727" data-file-width="2935" decoding="async" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Statue_de_L%27Empereur_Auguste_-_MR_99_-_Ma_1278.jpg/170px-Statue_de_L%27Empereur_Auguste_-_MR_99_-_Ma_1278.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Statue_de_L%27Empereur_Auguste_-_MR_99_-_Ma_1278.jpg/255px-Statue_de_L%27Empereur_Auguste_-_MR_99_-_Ma_1278.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Statue_de_L%27Empereur_Auguste_-_MR_99_-_Ma_1278.jpg/340px-Statue_de_L%27Empereur_Auguste_-_MR_99_-_Ma_1278.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Statue_de_L%27Empereur_Auguste_-_MR_99_-_Ma_1278.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Octavian as a magistrate. The statue's marble head was made <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 30–20 BC, the body sculpted in the 2nd century AD (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre" title="Musée du Louvre">Louvre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>).</div></div></div>
<p>According to historian <a href="/wiki/Howard_Hayes_Scullard" title="Howard Hayes Scullard">H. H. Scullard</a>, however, Octavian's power was based on the exercise of "a predominant military power and ... the ultimate sanction of his authority was force, however much the fact was disguised."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScullard1982211_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScullard1982211-133">[127]</a></sup> The Senate proposed to Octavian, the victor of Rome's civil wars, that he once again assume command of the provinces. The Senate's proposal was a ratification of Octavian's extra-constitutional power. Through the Senate, Octavian was able to continue the appearance of a still-functional <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Roman_Republic" title="Constitution of the Roman Republic">constitution</a>. Feigning reluctance, he accepted a ten-year responsibility of overseeing provinces that were considered chaotic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200346_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200346-134">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScullard1982210_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScullard1982210-135">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>The provinces ceded to Augustus for that ten-year period comprised much of the conquered Roman world, including all of Hispania and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Gaul" title="Roman Gaul">Gaul</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syria_(Roman_province)" title="Syria (Roman province)">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%86gyptus" title="Ægyptus">Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200346_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200346-134">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen200534_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen200534-136">[130]</a></sup> Moreover, command of these provinces provided Octavian with control over the majority of Rome's legions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen200534_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen200534-136">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200347_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200347-137">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>While Octavian acted as consul in Rome, he dispatched senators to the provinces under his command as his representatives to manage provincial affairs and ensure that his orders were carried out. The provinces not under Octavian's control were overseen by governors chosen by the Roman Senate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200347_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200347-137">[131]</a></sup> Octavian became the most powerful political figure in the city of Rome and in most of its provinces, but he did not have a monopoly on political and martial power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200524_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200524-138">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Senate still controlled North Africa, an important regional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_agriculture" title="Roman agriculture">producer of grain</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Illyria" title="Illyria">Illyria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(Roman_province)" title="Macedonia (Roman province)">Macedonia</a>, two strategic regions with several legions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200524_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200524-138">[132]</a></sup> However, the Senate had control of only five or six legions distributed among three senatorial proconsuls, compared to the twenty legions under the control of Octavian, and their control of these regions did not amount to any political or military challenge to Octavian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200524–25_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200524–25-126">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScullard1982211_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScullard1982211-133">[127]</a></sup> The Senate's control over some of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">Roman provinces</a> helped maintain a republican façade for the autocratic Principate. Also, Octavian's control of entire provinces followed Republican-era precedents for the objective of securing peace and creating stability, in which such prominent Romans as Pompey had been granted similar military powers in times of crisis and instability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200524–25_126-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200524–25-126">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Change_to_Augustus">Change to Augustus</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Augustus_first_century_aureus_obverse.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="353" data-file-width="358" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Augustus_first_century_aureus_obverse.png/220px-Augustus_first_century_aureus_obverse.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Augustus_first_century_aureus_obverse.png/330px-Augustus_first_century_aureus_obverse.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7a/Augustus_first_century_aureus_obverse.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Augustus_first_century_aureus_obverse.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aureus" title="Aureus">Aureus</a> minted <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> AD 13, marked: "Caesar Augustus Divi F Pater Patriae"</div></div></div>
<p>On 16 January 27 BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[137]</a></sup> the Senate gave Octavian the new titles of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augustus_(honorific)" title="Augustus (honorific)">Augustus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Princeps" title="Princeps">Princeps</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200350_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200350-144">[138]</a></sup> <i>Augustus</i> is from the Latin word <i>Augere</i> (meaning to increase) and can be translated as "the illustrious one". It was a title of religious authority rather than political authority. His new name of Augustus was also more favorable than <i>Romulus</i>, the previous one which he styled for himself in reference to the story of <a href="/wiki/Romulus" title="Romulus">the legendary founder of Rome</a>, which symbolized a second founding of Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200349_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200349-124">[118]</a></sup> The title of <i>Romulus</i> was associated too strongly with notions of monarchy and kingship, an image that Octavian tried to avoid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003149_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003149-145">[139]</a></sup> The title <i><a href="/wiki/Princeps_senatus" title="Princeps senatus">princeps senatus</a></i> originally meant the member of the Senate with the highest precedence,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[140]</a></sup> but in this case it became an almost regnal title for a leader who was first in charge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20033,_149_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20033,_149-147">[141]</a></sup> The name <i>Augustus</i> was inherited by all future emperors, eventually becoming, at least in practice, the main title of the emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[143]</a></sup> As a result, modern historians usually regard this event as the beginning of Augustus' reign as "emperor".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[g]</a></sup> Augustus also styled himself as <i>Imperator Caesar divi filius</i>, "Commander Caesar son of the deified one". With this title, he boasted his familial link to deified Julius Caesar, and the use of <i><a href="/wiki/Imperator" title="Imperator">Imperator</a></i> signified a permanent link to the Roman tradition of victory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[h]</a></sup> He transformed <i>Caesar</i>, a cognomen for one branch of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julius" title="Julius">Julian family</a>, into a new family line that began with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200350_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200350-144">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arch_of_Augustus_at_Ariminum,_dedicated_to_the_Emperor_Augustus_by_the_Roman_Senate_in_27_BC,_the_oldest_Roman_arch_which_survives,_Rimini,_Italy_(19948839545).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4718" data-file-width="3128" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Arch_of_Augustus_at_Ariminum%2C_dedicated_to_the_Emperor_Augustus_by_the_Roman_Senate_in_27_BC%2C_the_oldest_Roman_arch_which_survives%2C_Rimini%2C_Italy_%2819948839545%29.jpg/170px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Arch_of_Augustus_at_Ariminum%2C_dedicated_to_the_Emperor_Augustus_by_the_Roman_Senate_in_27_BC%2C_the_oldest_Roman_arch_which_survives%2C_Rimini%2C_Italy_%2819948839545%29.jpg/255px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Arch_of_Augustus_at_Ariminum%2C_dedicated_to_the_Emperor_Augustus_by_the_Roman_Senate_in_27_BC%2C_the_oldest_Roman_arch_which_survives%2C_Rimini%2C_Italy_%2819948839545%29.jpg/340px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arch_of_Augustus_at_Ariminum,_dedicated_to_the_Emperor_Augustus_by_the_Roman_Senate_in_27_BC,_the_oldest_Roman_arch_which_survives,_Rimini,_Italy_(19948839545).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Arch_of_Augustus_(Rimini)" title="Arch of Augustus (Rimini)">Arch of Augustus</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rimini" title="Rimini">Rimini</a> (Ariminum), dedicated to Augustus by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Roman Senate</a> in 27 BC, one of the oldest surviving <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_triumphal_arches" title="List of Roman triumphal arches">Roman triumphal arches</a></div></div></div>
<p>Augustus was granted the right to hang the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corona_civica" title="Corona civica">corona civica</a></i> above his door, the "civic crown" made from oak, and to have laurels drape his doorposts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200524_138-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200524-138">[132]</a></sup> However, he renounced flaunting insignia of power such as holding a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scepter" title="Scepter">scepter</a>, wearing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diadem_(personal_wear)" title="Diadem (personal wear)">diadem</a>, or wearing the golden crown and purple <a href="/wiki/Toga" title="Toga">toga</a> of his predecessor Julius Caesar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200513_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200513-157">[149]</a></sup> If he refused to symbolize his power by donning and bearing these items on his person, the Senate nonetheless awarded him with a golden shield displayed in the meeting hall of the <a href="/wiki/Curia" title="Curia">Curia</a>, bearing the inscription <i>virtus</i>, <i>pietas</i>, <i>clementia</i>, <i>iustitia</i>—"valor, piety, clemency, and justice."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200524_138-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200524-138">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20033_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20033-158">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_settlement">Second settlement</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roman_-_Portrait_of_Emperor_Augustus_-_Walters_2321.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1799" data-file-width="1160" decoding="async" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Roman_-_Portrait_of_Emperor_Augustus_-_Walters_2321.jpg/170px-Roman_-_Portrait_of_Emperor_Augustus_-_Walters_2321.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Roman_-_Portrait_of_Emperor_Augustus_-_Walters_2321.jpg/255px-Roman_-_Portrait_of_Emperor_Augustus_-_Walters_2321.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Roman_-_Portrait_of_Emperor_Augustus_-_Walters_2321.jpg/340px-Roman_-_Portrait_of_Emperor_Augustus_-_Walters_2321.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roman_-_Portrait_of_Emperor_Augustus_-_Walters_2321.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portraits of Augustus show the emperor with idealized features</div></div></div>
<p>By 23 BC, some of the un-Republican implications were becoming apparent concerning the settlement of 27 BC. Augustus's retention of an annual consulate drew attention to his <i>de facto</i> dominance over the Roman political system, and cut in half the opportunities for others to achieve what was still nominally the preeminent position in the Roman state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells200451_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells200451-159">[151]</a></sup> Further, he was causing political problems by desiring to have his nephew <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Claudius_Marcellus_(nephew_of_Augustus)" title="Marcus Claudius Marcellus (nephew of Augustus)">Marcus Claudius Marcellus</a> follow in his footsteps and eventually assume the Principate in his turn,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[i]</a></sup> alienating his three greatest supporters – Agrippa, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maecenas" title="Maecenas">Maecenas</a>, and Livia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolland2005294_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolland2005294-163">[154]</a></sup> He appointed noted Republican <a href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Calpurnius_Piso_(consul_23_BC)" title="Gnaeus Calpurnius Piso (consul 23 BC)">Calpurnius Piso</a> (who had fought against Julius Caesar and supported Cassius and Brutus<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies2010259_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies2010259-164">[155]</a></sup>) as co-consul in 23 BC, after his choice <a href="/wiki/Aulus_Terentius_Varro_Murena" title="Aulus Terentius Varro Murena">Aulus Terentius Varro Murena</a> died unexpectedly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndo2000140_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndo2000140-165">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaaflaubSamons1993426_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaaflaubSamons1993426-166">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells200453_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells200453-167">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late spring Augustus had a severe illness, and on his supposed deathbed made arrangements that would ensure the continuation of the Principate in some form,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESouthern1998108_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESouthern1998108-160">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolland2005295_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolland2005295-168">[159]</a></sup> while allaying senators' suspicions of his anti-republicanism. Augustus prepared to hand down his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seal_(device)#Signet_rings" title="Seal (device)">signet ring</a> to his favored general Agrippa. However, Augustus handed over to his co-consul Piso all of his official documents, an account of public finances, and authority over listed troops in the provinces while Augustus's supposedly favored nephew <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Claudius_Marcellus_(nephew_of_Augustus)" title="Marcus Claudius Marcellus (nephew of Augustus)">Marcellus</a> came away empty-handed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200525_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200525-169">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200356_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200356-170">[161]</a></sup> This was a surprise to many who believed Augustus would have named an heir to his position as an unofficial emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen200538_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen200538-171">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>Augustus bestowed only properties and possessions to his designated heirs, as an obvious system of institutionalized imperial inheritance would have provoked resistance and hostility among the republican-minded Romans fearful of monarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen200538–39_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen200538–39-127">[121]</a></sup> With regards to the Principate, it was obvious to Augustus that Marcellus was not ready to take on his position;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern,_Gaius_p._23_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stern,_Gaius_p._23-172">[163]</a></sup> nonetheless, by giving his signet ring to Agrippa, Augustus intended to signal to the legions that Agrippa was to be his successor, and that constitutional procedure notwithstanding, they should continue to obey Agrippa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolland2005294–295_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolland2005294–295-173">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESouthern1998108_160-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESouthern1998108-160">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cameo_August_BM_Gem3577.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3200" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Cameo_August_BM_Gem3577.jpg/170px-Cameo_August_BM_Gem3577.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Cameo_August_BM_Gem3577.jpg/255px-Cameo_August_BM_Gem3577.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Cameo_August_BM_Gem3577.jpg/340px-Cameo_August_BM_Gem3577.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cameo_August_BM_Gem3577.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Blacas_Cameo" title="Blacas Cameo">Blacas Cameo</a> showing Augustus wearing a <i><a href="/wiki/Gorgoneion" title="Gorgoneion">gorgoneion</a></i> on a three layered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sardonyx" title="Sardonyx">sardonyx</a> cameo, AD 20–50</div></div></div>
<p>Soon after his bout of illness subsided, Augustus gave up his consulship. The only other times Augustus would serve as consul would be in the years 5 and 2 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200356_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200356-170">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200526_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200526-174">[165]</a></sup> both times to introduce his grandsons into public life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies2010259_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies2010259-164">[155]</a></sup> This was a clever ploy by Augustus; ceasing to serve as one of two annually elected consuls allowed aspiring senators a better chance to attain the consular position, while allowing Augustus to exercise wider patronage within the senatorial class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen200536_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen200536-175">[166]</a></sup> Although Augustus had resigned as consul, he desired to retain his consular <i><a href="/wiki/Imperium" title="Imperium">imperium</a></i> not just in his provinces but throughout the empire. This desire, as well as the Marcus Primus Affair, led to a second compromise between him and the Senate known as the Second Settlement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200357_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200357-176">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_reasons_for_the_Second_settlement">Primary reasons for the Second settlement</span></h3>
<p>The primary reasons for the Second Settlement were as follows. First, after Augustus relinquished the annual consulship, he was no longer in an official position to rule the state, yet his dominant position remained unchanged over his Roman, 'imperial' provinces where he was still a <a href="/wiki/Proconsul" title="Proconsul">proconsul</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200356_170-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200356-170">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen200537_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen200537-177">[168]</a></sup> When he annually held the office of consul, he had the power to intervene with the affairs of the other provincial proconsuls appointed by the Senate throughout the empire, when he deemed necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200356–57_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200356–57-178">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>A second problem later arose showing the need for the Second Settlement in what became known as the "Marcus Primus Affair".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESouthern1998109_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESouthern1998109-179">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolland2005299_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolland2005299-180">[171]</a></sup> In late 24 or early 23 BC, charges were brought against Marcus Primus, the former proconsul (governor) of Macedonia, for waging a war without prior approval of the Senate on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Odrysian" title="Odrysian">Odrysian</a> kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a>, whose king was a Roman ally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells200453_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells200453-167">[158]</a></sup> He was defended by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucius_Lucinius_Varro_Murena" title="Lucius Lucinius Varro Murena">Lucius Lucinius Varro Murena</a>, who told the trial that his client had received specific instructions from Augustus, ordering him to attack the client state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESouthern1998108_160-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESouthern1998108-160">[152]</a></sup> Later, Primus testified that the orders came from the recently deceased Marcellus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolland2005300_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolland2005300-181">[172]</a></sup> Such orders, had they been given, would have been considered a breach of the Senate's prerogative under the Constitutional settlement of 27 BC and its aftermath – i.e., before Augustus was granted <i>imperium proconsulare maius</i> – as Macedonia was a Senatorial province under the Senate's jurisdiction, not an imperial province under the authority of Augustus. Such an action would have ripped away the veneer of Republican restoration as promoted by Augustus, and exposed his fraud of merely being the first citizen, a first among equals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESouthern1998108_160-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESouthern1998108-160">[152]</a></sup> Even worse, the involvement of Marcellus provided some measure of proof that Augustus's policy was to have the youth take his place as Princeps, instituting a form of monarchy – accusations that had already played out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern,_Gaius_p._23_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stern,_Gaius_p._23-172">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_the_Emperor_Octavian_Augustus_as_Jupiter_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3676" data-file-width="2536" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Statue_of_the_Emperor_Octavian_Augustus_as_Jupiter_1.jpg/170px-Statue_of_the_Emperor_Octavian_Augustus_as_Jupiter_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Statue_of_the_Emperor_Octavian_Augustus_as_Jupiter_1.jpg/255px-Statue_of_the_Emperor_Octavian_Augustus_as_Jupiter_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Statue_of_the_Emperor_Octavian_Augustus_as_Jupiter_1.jpg/340px-Statue_of_the_Emperor_Octavian_Augustus_as_Jupiter_1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_the_Emperor_Octavian_Augustus_as_Jupiter_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Augustus as <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a>, holding a scepter and orb (first half of 1st century AD)</div></div></div>
<p>The situation was so serious that Augustus himself appeared at the trial, even though he had not been called as a witness. Under oath, Augustus declared that he gave no such order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESyme1939333_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESyme1939333-182">[173]</a></sup> Murena disbelieved Augustus's testimony and resented his attempt to subvert the trial by using his <i><a href="/wiki/Auctoritas" title="Auctoritas">auctoritas</a></i>. He rudely demanded to know why Augustus had turned up to a trial to which he had not been called; Augustus replied that he came in the public interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESyme1939333_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESyme1939333-182">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolland2005300_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolland2005300-181">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESouthern1998108_160-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESouthern1998108-160">[152]</a></sup> Although Primus was found guilty, some jurors voted to acquit, meaning that not everybody believed Augustus's testimony, an insult to the 'August One'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells200453_167-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells200453-167">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaaflaubSamons1993426_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaaflaubSamons1993426-166">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Second Constitutional Settlement was completed in part to allay confusion and formalize Augustus's legal authority to intervene in Senatorial provinces. The Senate granted Augustus a form of general <i>imperium proconsulare</i>, or proconsular imperium (power) that applied throughout the empire, not solely to his provinces. Moreover, the Senate augmented Augustus's proconsular imperium into <i>imperium proconsulare maius</i>, or proconsular imperium applicable throughout the empire that was more (maius) or greater than that held by the other proconsuls. This in effect gave Augustus constitutional power superior to all other proconsuls in the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200357_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200357-176">[167]</a></sup> Augustus stayed in Rome during the renewal process and provided veterans with lavish donations to gain their support, thereby ensuring that his status of proconsular imperium maius was renewed in 13 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200526_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200526-174">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Additional_powers">Additional powers</span></h3>
<p>During the Second settlement, Augustus was also granted the power of a <a href="/wiki/Tribune_of_the_plebs" title="Tribune of the plebs">tribune</a> (<i>tribunicia potestas</i>) for life, though not the official title of tribune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200357_176-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200357-176">[167]</a></sup> For some years, Augustus had been awarded <i>tribunicia sacrosanctitas</i>, the immunity given to a <a href="/wiki/Tribune_of_the_plebs" title="Tribune of the plebs">tribune of the plebs</a>. Now he decided to assume the full powers of the magistracy, renewed annually, in perpetuity. Legally, it was closed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patrician_(Ancient_Rome)" title="Patrician (Ancient Rome)">patricians</a>, a status that Augustus had acquired some years earlier when adopted by Julius Caesar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen200536_175-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen200536-175">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>This power allowed him to convene the Senate and people at will and lay business before them, to veto the actions of either the Assembly or the Senate, to preside over elections, and to speak first at any meeting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200526_174-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200526-174">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200357–58_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200357–58-183">[174]</a></sup> Also included in Augustus's tribunician authority were powers usually reserved for the <a href="/wiki/Roman_censor" title="Roman censor">Roman censor</a>; these included the right to supervise public morals and scrutinize laws to ensure that they were in the public interest, as well as the ability to hold a <a href="/wiki/Census" title="Census">census</a> and determine the membership of the Senate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200359_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200359-184">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:August_com_a_%22Pontifex_maximus%22_(detall)_(finals_s._I_aC),_Museu_Nacional_Rom%C3%A0_(Palau_Massimo),_Roma.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/August_com_a_%22Pontifex_maximus%22_%28detall%29_%28finals_s._I_aC%29%2C_Museu_Nacional_Rom%C3%A0_%28Palau_Massimo%29%2C_Roma.jpg/170px-August_com_a_%22Pontifex_maximus%22_%28detall%29_%28finals_s._I_aC%29%2C_Museu_Nacional_Rom%C3%A0_%28Palau_Massimo%29%2C_Roma.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/August_com_a_%22Pontifex_maximus%22_%28detall%29_%28finals_s._I_aC%29%2C_Museu_Nacional_Rom%C3%A0_%28Palau_Massimo%29%2C_Roma.jpg/255px-August_com_a_%22Pontifex_maximus%22_%28detall%29_%28finals_s._I_aC%29%2C_Museu_Nacional_Rom%C3%A0_%28Palau_Massimo%29%2C_Roma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/August_com_a_%22Pontifex_maximus%22_%28detall%29_%28finals_s._I_aC%29%2C_Museu_Nacional_Rom%C3%A0_%28Palau_Massimo%29%2C_Roma.jpg/340px-August_com_a_%22Pontifex_maximus%22_%28detall%29_%28finals_s._I_aC%29%2C_Museu_Nacional_Rom%C3%A0_%28Palau_Massimo%29%2C_Roma.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:August_com_a_%22Pontifex_maximus%22_(detall)_(finals_s._I_aC),_Museu_Nacional_Rom%C3%A0_(Palau_Massimo),_Roma.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Via_Labicana_Augustus" title="Via Labicana Augustus">Head of Augustus</a> as <i><a href="/wiki/Pontifex_maximus" title="Pontifex maximus">pontifex maximus</a></i>, Roman artwork of the late Augustan period, last decade of the 1st century BC</div></div></div>
<p>With the powers of a censor, Augustus appealed to virtues of Roman patriotism by banning all attire but the classic <a href="/wiki/Toga" title="Toga">toga</a> while entering the Forum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200530_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200530-185">[176]</a></sup> There was no precedent within the Roman system for combining the powers of the tribune and the censor into a single position, nor was Augustus ever elected to the office of censor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson199480_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson199480-186">[177]</a></sup> Julius Caesar had been granted similar powers, wherein he was charged with supervising the morals of the state. However, this position did not extend to the censor's ability to hold a census and determine the Senate's roster. The office of the <i>tribunus plebis</i> began to lose its prestige due to Augustus's amassing of tribunal powers, so he revived its importance by making it a mandatory appointment for any plebeian desiring the <a href="/wiki/Praetor" title="Praetor">praetorship</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson1994427_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson1994427-187">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p>Augustus was granted sole <i>imperium</i> within the city of Rome itself, in addition to being granted proconsular <i>imperium maius</i> and tribunician authority for life. Traditionally, proconsuls (Roman province governors) lost their proconsular "imperium" when they crossed the <a href="/wiki/Pomerium" title="Pomerium">Pomerium</a> – the sacred boundary of Rome – and entered the city. In these situations, Augustus would have power as part of his tribunician authority but his constitutional imperium within the Pomerium would be less than that of a serving consul. That would mean that, when he was in the city, he might not be the constitutional magistrate with the most authority. Thanks to his prestige or <i>auctoritas</i>, his wishes would usually be obeyed, but there might be some difficulty. To fill this power vacuum, the Senate voted that Augustus's <i>imperium proconsulare maius</i> (superior proconsular power) should not lapse when he was inside the city walls. All armed forces in the city had formerly been under the control of the urban praetors and consuls, but this situation now placed them under the sole authority of Augustus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200360_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200360-188">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, the credit was given to Augustus for each subsequent Roman military victory after this time, because the majority of Rome's armies were stationed in imperial provinces commanded by Augustus through the <a href="/wiki/Legatus" title="Legatus">legatus</a> who were deputies of the princeps in the provinces. Moreover, if a battle was fought in a Senatorial province, Augustus's proconsular imperium maius allowed him to take command of (or credit for) any major military victory. This meant that Augustus was the only individual able to receive a <a href="/wiki/Roman_triumph" title="Roman triumph">triumph</a>, a tradition that began with Romulus, Rome's first King and first triumphant general. <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Cornelius_Balbus_(proconsul)" title="Lucius Cornelius Balbus (proconsul)">Lucius Cornelius Balbus</a> was the last man outside Augustus's family to receive this award, in 19 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200361_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200361-189">[180]</a></sup> Tiberius, Augustus's eldest stepson by Livia, was the only other general to receive a triumph—for victories in Germania in 7 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003117_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003117-190">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>Normally during republican times, the powers Augustus held even after the Second Settlement would have been split between several people, who would each exercise them with the assistance of a colleague and for a specific period of time. Augustus held them all at once by himself, and with no time limits; even those that nominally had time limits were automatically renewed whenever they lapsed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[182]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conspiracy">Conspiracy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Augustus_MAN_Napoli_Inv6040.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4079" data-file-width="2720" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Augustus_MAN_Napoli_Inv6040.jpg/170px-Augustus_MAN_Napoli_Inv6040.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Augustus_MAN_Napoli_Inv6040.jpg/255px-Augustus_MAN_Napoli_Inv6040.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Augustus_MAN_Napoli_Inv6040.jpg/340px-Augustus_MAN_Napoli_Inv6040.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Augustus_MAN_Napoli_Inv6040.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A colossal statue of Augustus from the <a href="/wiki/Sodales_Augustales" title="Sodales Augustales">Augusteum</a> of <a href="/wiki/Herculaneum" title="Herculaneum">Herculaneum</a>, seated and wearing a <a href="/wiki/Laurel_wreath" title="Laurel wreath">laurel wreath</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Many of the political subtleties of the Second Settlement seem to have evaded the comprehension of the Plebeian class, who were Augustus's greatest supporters and clientele. This caused them to insist upon Augustus's participation in imperial affairs from time to time. Augustus failed to stand for election as consul in 22 BC, and fears arose once again that he was being forced from power by the aristocratic Senate. In 22, 21, and 19 BC, the people rioted in response, and only allowed a single consul to be elected for each of those years, ostensibly to leave the other position open for Augustus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassius_Dio[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsCassius_Dio54html1_54.1],_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsCassius_Dio54html6_6],_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsCassius_Dio54html10_10]_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassius_Dio[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsCassius_Dio54html1_54.1],_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsCassius_Dio54html6_6],_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsCassius_Dio54html10_10]-192">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p>Likewise, there was a food shortage in Rome in 22 BC which sparked panic, while many urban plebs called for Augustus to take on dictatorial powers to personally oversee the crisis. After a theatrical display of refusal before the Senate, Augustus finally accepted authority over Rome's grain supply "by virtue of his proconsular <i>imperium</i>", and ended the crisis almost immediately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200526_174-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200526-174">[165]</a></sup> It was not until AD 8 that a food crisis of this sort prompted Augustus to establish a <i><a href="/wiki/Praefectus_annonae" title="Praefectus annonae">praefectus annonae</a></i>, a permanent prefect who was in charge of procuring food supplies for Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200378_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200378-193">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>There were some who were concerned by the expansion of powers granted to Augustus by the Second Settlement, and this came to a head with the apparent conspiracy of Fannius Caepio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESouthern1998109_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESouthern1998109-179">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolland2005299_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolland2005299-180">[171]</a></sup> Some time prior to 1 September 22 BC, a certain Castricius provided Augustus with information about a conspiracy led by Fannius Caepio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESyme1939483_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESyme1939483-195">[186]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucius_Lucinius_Varro_Murena" title="Lucius Lucinius Varro Murena">Murena</a>, the outspoken Consul who defended Primus in the Marcus Primus Affair, was named among the conspirators. The conspirators were tried in absentia with Tiberius acting as prosecutor; the jury found them guilty, but it was not a unanimous verdict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells200453_167-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells200453-167">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolland2005301_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolland2005301-196">[187]</a></sup> All the accused were sentenced to death for treason and executed as soon as they were captured—without ever giving testimony in their defence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies2010260_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies2010260-197">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolland2005301_196-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolland2005301-196">[187]</a></sup> Augustus ensured that the facade of Republican government continued with an effective cover-up of the events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolland2005301_196-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolland2005301-196">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 19 BC, the Senate granted Augustus a form of 'general consular imperium', which was probably 'imperium consulare maius', like the proconsular powers that he received in 23 BC. Like his tribune authority, the consular powers were another instance of gaining power from offices that he did not actually hold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen200543_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen200543-198">[189]</a></sup> In addition, Augustus was allowed to wear the consul's insignia in public and before the Senate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200360_188-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200360-188">[179]</a></sup> as well as to sit in the symbolic chair between the two consuls and hold the <a href="/wiki/Fasces" title="Fasces">fasces</a>, an emblem of consular authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen200543_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen200543-198">[189]</a></sup> This seems to have assuaged the populace; regardless of whether or not Augustus was a consul, the importance was that he both appeared as one before the people and could exercise consular power if necessary. On 6 March 12 BC, after the death of <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aemilius_Lepidus_(triumvir)" title="Marcus Aemilius Lepidus (triumvir)">Lepidus</a>, he additionally took up the position of <i>pontifex maximus</i>, the high priest of the college of the Pontiffs, the most important position in Roman religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[j]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[k]</a></sup> On 5 February 2 BC, Augustus was also given the title <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pater_patriae" title="Pater patriae">pater patriae</a></i>, or "father of the country".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMackay2004186_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMackay2004186-205">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003129_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003129-206">[195]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stability_and_staying_power">Stability and staying power</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glyptothek_M%C3%BCnchen_%E2%80%93_18.04.2022_%E2%80%93_Augustus_Bevilacqua_(4).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7645" data-file-width="4905" decoding="async" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Glyptothek_M%C3%BCnchen_%E2%80%93_18.04.2022_%E2%80%93_Augustus_Bevilacqua_%284%29.jpg/170px-Glyptothek_M%C3%BCnchen_%E2%80%93_18.04.2022_%E2%80%93_Augustus_Bevilacqua_%284%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Glyptothek_M%C3%BCnchen_%E2%80%93_18.04.2022_%E2%80%93_Augustus_Bevilacqua_%284%29.jpg/255px-Glyptothek_M%C3%BCnchen_%E2%80%93_18.04.2022_%E2%80%93_Augustus_Bevilacqua_%284%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Glyptothek_M%C3%BCnchen_%E2%80%93_18.04.2022_%E2%80%93_Augustus_Bevilacqua_%284%29.jpg/340px-Glyptothek_M%C3%BCnchen_%E2%80%93_18.04.2022_%E2%80%93_Augustus_Bevilacqua_%284%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glyptothek_M%C3%BCnchen_%E2%80%93_18.04.2022_%E2%80%93_Augustus_Bevilacqua_(4).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of Augustus wearing the <a href="/wiki/Civic_Crown" title="Civic Crown">Civic Crown</a>, at <a href="/wiki/Glyptothek" title="Glyptothek">Glyptothek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a></div></div></div>
<p>A final reason for the Second Settlement was to give the Principate constitutional stability and staying power in case something happened to Princeps Augustus. His illness of early 23 BC and the Caepio conspiracy showed that the regime's existence hung by the thin thread of the life of one man, Augustus himself, who had several severe and dangerous illnesses throughout his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[196]</a></sup> If he were to die from natural causes or fall victim to assassination, Rome could be subjected to another round of civil war. The memories of Pharsalus, the Ides of March, the proscriptions, Philippi, and Actium, barely twenty-five years distant, were still vivid in the minds of many citizens. Proconsular imperium was conferred upon <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Vipsanius_Agrippa" title="Marcus Vipsanius Agrippa">Agrippa</a> for five years, similar to Augustus's power, in order to accomplish this constitutional stability. The exact nature of the grant is uncertain but it probably covered Augustus's imperial provinces, east and west, perhaps lacking authority over the provinces of the Senate. That came later, as did the jealously guarded tribunicia potestas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESyme1939337–338_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESyme1939337–338-208">[197]</a></sup> Augustus's accumulation of powers was now complete.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_and_expansion">War and expansion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Augustus" title="Wars of Augustus">Wars of Augustus</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Iranian_relations" title="Roman–Iranian relations">Roman–Iranian relations</a></div>
<p>Augustus chose <i>Imperator</i> ("victorious commander") to be his first name, since he wanted to make an emphatically clear connection between himself and the notion of victory, and consequently became known as <i>Imperator Caesar Divi Filius Augustus</i>. By the year 13, Augustus boasted 21 occasions where his troops proclaimed "imperator" as his title after a successful battle. Almost the entire fourth chapter in his publicly released memoirs of achievements known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Res_Gestae_Divi_Augusti" title="Res Gestae Divi Augusti">Res Gestae</a></i> was devoted to his military victories and honors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200393_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200393-209">[198]</a></sup>
</p><p>Augustus also promoted the ideal of a superior Roman civilization with a task of ruling the world (to the extent to which the Romans knew it), a sentiment embodied in words that the contemporary poet <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a> attributes to a legendary ancestor of Augustus: <i>tu regere imperio populos, Romane, memento</i>—"Roman, remember by your strength to rule the Earth's peoples!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200530_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200530-185">[176]</a></sup> The impulse for <a href="/wiki/Expansionism" title="Expansionism">expansionism</a> was apparently prominent among all classes at Rome, and it is accorded divine sanction by Virgil's Jupiter in Book 1 of the <i><a href="/wiki/Aeneid" title="Aeneid">Aeneid</a></i>, where Jupiter promises Rome <i>imperium sine fine</i>, "sovereignty without end".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200395_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200395-210">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the end of his reign, the armies of Augustus had conquered northern <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a> (modern Spain and Portugal) and the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alpine</a> regions of <a href="/wiki/Raetia" title="Raetia">Raetia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Noricum" title="Noricum">Noricum</a> (modern Switzerland, Bavaria, Austria, Slovenia), <a href="/wiki/Illyricum_(Roman_province)" title="Illyricum (Roman province)">Illyricum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pannonia" title="Pannonia">Pannonia</a> (modern Albania, Croatia, Hungary, Serbia, etc.), and had extended the borders of <a href="/wiki/Africa_(Roman_province)" title="Africa (Roman province)">Africa Proconsularis</a> to the east and south. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iudaea_Province" title="Iudaea Province">Judea</a> was added to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syria_(Roman_province)" title="Syria (Roman province)">province of Syria</a> when Augustus deposed <a href="/wiki/Herod_Archelaus" title="Herod Archelaus">Herod Archelaus</a>, successor to <a href="/wiki/Client_state" title="Client state">client king</a> <a href="/wiki/Herod_the_Great" title="Herod the Great">Herod the Great</a> (73–4 BC). Syria (like Egypt after Antony) was governed by a high prefect of the equestrian class rather than by a proconsul or legate of Augustus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200394_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200394-211">[200]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tiberius_NyCarlsberg01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2093" data-file-width="1492" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Tiberius_NyCarlsberg01.jpg/170px-Tiberius_NyCarlsberg01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Tiberius_NyCarlsberg01.jpg/255px-Tiberius_NyCarlsberg01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Tiberius_NyCarlsberg01.jpg/340px-Tiberius_NyCarlsberg01.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tiberius_NyCarlsberg01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a>, a successful military commander under Augustus before he was designated as his heir and successor</div></div></div>
<p>Again, no military effort was needed in 25 BC when <a href="/wiki/Galatia" title="Galatia">Galatia</a> (modern Turkey) was converted to a Roman province shortly after <a href="/wiki/Amyntas_of_Galatia" title="Amyntas of Galatia">Amyntas of Galatia</a> was killed by an avenging widow of a slain prince from Homonada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200394_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200394-211">[200]</a></sup> The rebellious tribes of <a href="/wiki/Asturias" title="Asturias">Asturias</a> and  <a href="/wiki/Cantabria" title="Cantabria">Cantabria</a> in modern-day Spain were <a href="/wiki/Cantabrian_Wars" title="Cantabrian Wars">finally quelled in 19 BC</a>, and the territory fell under the provinces of Hispania and <a href="/wiki/Lusitania" title="Lusitania">Lusitania</a>. This region proved to be a major asset in funding Augustus's future military campaigns, as it was rich in mineral deposits that could be fostered in Roman <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mining</a> projects, especially the very rich <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> deposits at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Las_Medulas" title="Las Medulas">Las Medulas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200397_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200397-212">[201]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TabulaPeutingerianaMuziris.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="373" data-file-width="457" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/TabulaPeutingerianaMuziris.jpg/220px-TabulaPeutingerianaMuziris.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/TabulaPeutingerianaMuziris.jpg/330px-TabulaPeutingerianaMuziris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/TabulaPeutingerianaMuziris.jpg/440px-TabulaPeutingerianaMuziris.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TabulaPeutingerianaMuziris.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Muziris" title="Muziris">Muziris</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chera_Kingdom" title="Chera Kingdom">Chera Kingdom</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_India" title="Southern India">Southern India</a>, as shown in the <a href="/wiki/Tabula_Peutingeriana" title="Tabula Peutingeriana">Tabula Peutingeriana</a>, with depiction of a "Temple of Augustus" ("Templum Augusti"), an illustration of <a href="/wiki/Indo-Roman_relations" title="Indo-Roman relations">Indo-Roman relations</a> in the period</div></div></div>
<p>Conquering the peoples of the Alps in 16 BC was another important victory for Rome, since it provided a large territorial buffer between the Roman citizens of Italy and Rome's enemies in <a href="/wiki/Germania" title="Germania">Germania</a> to the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200398_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200398-213">[202]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Horace" title="Horace">Horace</a> dedicated an ode to the victory, while the monumental <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trophy_of_Augustus" title="Trophy of Augustus">Trophy of Augustus</a> near <a href="/wiki/Monaco" title="Monaco">Monaco</a> was built to honor the occasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200398–99_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200398–99-214">[203]</a></sup> The capture of the Alpine region also served the next offensive in 12 BC, when Tiberius began the offensive against the Pannonian tribes of Illyricum, and his brother <a href="/wiki/Nero_Claudius_Drusus" title="Nero Claudius Drusus">Nero Claudius Drusus</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Imperial_campaigns_in_Germania" title="Early Imperial campaigns in Germania">moved against the Germanic tribes</a> of the eastern <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">Rhineland</a>. Both campaigns were successful, as Drusus's forces reached the <a href="/wiki/Elbe" title="Elbe">Elbe</a> River by 9 BC—though he died shortly after by falling off his horse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200399_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200399-215">[204]</a></sup> It was recorded that the pious Tiberius walked in front of his brother's body all the way back to Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson1994416_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson1994416-216">[205]</a></sup>
</p><p>To protect Rome's eastern territories from the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a>, Augustus relied on the <a href="/wiki/Client_state" title="Client state">client states</a> of the east to act as territorial <a href="/wiki/Buffer_state" title="Buffer state">buffers</a> and areas that could raise their own troops for defense. To ensure security of the Empire's eastern flank, Augustus stationed a Roman army in Syria, while his skilled stepson Tiberius negotiated with the Parthians as Rome's diplomat to the East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200396_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200396-217">[206]</a></sup> Tiberius was responsible for restoring <a href="/wiki/Tigranes_V_of_Armenia" title="Tigranes V of Armenia">Tigranes V</a> to the throne of the Kingdom of Armenia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson1994416_216-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson1994416-216">[205]</a></sup>
</p><p>Yet arguably his greatest diplomatic achievement was negotiating with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phraates_IV_of_Parthia" title="Phraates IV of Parthia">Phraates IV of Parthia</a> (37–2 BC) in 20 BC for the return of the <a href="/wiki/Vexilloid" title="Vexilloid">battle standards</a> lost by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crassus" title="Crassus">Crassus</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carrhae" title="Battle of Carrhae">Battle of Carrhae</a>, a symbolic victory and great boost of morale for Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson1994416_216-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson1994416-216">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200396_217-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200396-217">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrosius200696–97,_136–138_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrosius200696–97,_136–138-218">[207]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Werner_Eck" title="Werner Eck">Werner Eck</a> claims that this was a great disappointment for Romans seeking to avenge Crassus's defeat by military means.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200395–96_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200395–96-219">[208]</a></sup> However, Maria Brosius explains that Augustus used the return of the standards as <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a> symbolizing the submission of Parthia to Rome. The event was celebrated in art such as the breastplate design on the statue <a href="/wiki/Augustus_of_Prima_Porta" title="Augustus of Prima Porta">Augustus of Prima Porta</a> and in monuments such as the <a href="/wiki/Forum_of_Augustus" title="Forum of Augustus">Temple of Mars Ultor</a> ('<a href="/wiki/Mars_(mythology)" title="Mars (mythology)">Mars the Avenger</a>') built to house the standards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrosius200697_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrosius200697-220">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBivar198366–67_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBivar198366–67-221">[210]</a></sup>
</p><p>Parthia had always posed a threat to Rome in the east, but the real battlefront was along the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> rivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200396_217-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200396-217">[206]</a></sup> Before the final fight with Antony, Octavian's campaigns against the tribes in <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia_(Roman_province)" title="Dalmatia (Roman province)">Dalmatia</a> were the first step in expanding Roman dominions to the Danube.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowell196213_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowell196213-222">[211]</a></sup> Victory in battle was not always a permanent success, as newly conquered territories were constantly retaken by Rome's enemies in Germania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200396_217-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200396-217">[206]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hermann_warrior.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1224" data-file-width="1519" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Hermann_warrior.jpg/220px-Hermann_warrior.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Hermann_warrior.jpg/330px-Hermann_warrior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Hermann_warrior.jpg/440px-Hermann_warrior.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hermann_warrior.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The victorious advance of <a href="/wiki/Arminius" title="Arminius">Hermann</a></i>, depiction of the 9 AD <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Teutoburg_Forest" title="Battle of the Teutoburg Forest">Battle of the Teutoburg Forest</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Janssen" title="Peter Janssen">Peter Janssen</a>, 1873</div></div></div>
<p>A prime example of Roman loss in battle was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Teutoburg_Forest" title="Battle of Teutoburg Forest">Battle of Teutoburg Forest</a> in AD 9, where three entire legions led by <a href="/wiki/Publius_Quinctilius_Varus" title="Publius Quinctilius Varus">Publius Quinctilius Varus</a> were destroyed by <a href="/wiki/Arminius" title="Arminius">Arminius</a>, leader of the <a href="/wiki/Cherusci" title="Cherusci">Cherusci</a>, an apparent Roman ally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003101–102_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003101–102-223">[212]</a></sup> Augustus retaliated by dispatching Tiberius and Drusus to the Rhineland to pacify it, which had some success although the battle of AD 9 brought the end to Roman expansion into Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson1994417_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson1994417-224">[213]</a></sup> Roman general <a href="/wiki/Germanicus" title="Germanicus">Germanicus</a> took advantage of a Cherusci civil war between Arminius and <a href="/wiki/Segestes" title="Segestes">Segestes</a>; at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Idistaviso" title="Battle of Idistaviso">Battle of Idistaviso</a> in AD 16, they defeated Arminius, who fled but was killed later in 21 due to treachery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson199431_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson199431-225">[214]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_succession">Death and succession</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Octavianus_Caesar_Augustus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2803" data-file-width="1988" decoding="async" height="310" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Octavianus_Caesar_Augustus.jpg/220px-Octavianus_Caesar_Augustus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Octavianus_Caesar_Augustus.jpg/330px-Octavianus_Caesar_Augustus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Octavianus_Caesar_Augustus.jpg/440px-Octavianus_Caesar_Augustus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Octavianus_Caesar_Augustus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Augustus in a late 16th-century copper engraving by Giovanni Battista Cavalieri. From the book <i>Romanorum Imperatorum effigies</i> (1583), preserved in the Municipal Library of Trento (Italy)</div></div></div>
<p>The illness of Augustus in 23 BC brought the problem of succession to the forefront of political issues and the public. To ensure stability, he needed to designate an heir to his unique position in Roman society and government. This was to be achieved in small, undramatic, and incremental ways that did not stir senatorial fears of monarchy. If someone was to succeed to Augustus's unofficial position of power, he would have to earn it through his own publicly proven merits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen200550_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen200550-226">[215]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some Augustan historians argue that indications pointed toward his sister's son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Claudius_Marcellus_(nephew_of_Augustus)" title="Marcus Claudius Marcellus (nephew of Augustus)">Marcellus</a>, who had been quickly married to Augustus's daughter <a href="/wiki/Julia_the_Elder" title="Julia the Elder">Julia the Elder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003114–115_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003114–115-227">[216]</a></sup> Other historians dispute this due to Augustus's will being read aloud to the Senate while he was seriously ill in 23 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003115_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003115-228">[217]</a></sup> instead indicating a preference for Marcus Agrippa, who was Augustus's second in charge and arguably the only one of his associates who could have controlled the legions and held the Empire together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen200544_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen200544-229">[218]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the death of Marcellus in 23 BC, Augustus married his daughter to Agrippa. This union produced five children, three sons and two daughters: <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Caesar" title="Gaius Caesar">Gaius Caesar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Caesar" title="Lucius Caesar">Lucius Caesar</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vipsania_Julia" title="Vipsania Julia">Vipsania Julia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agrippina_the_Elder" title="Agrippina the Elder">Agrippina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Agrippa_Postumus" title="Agrippa Postumus">Agrippa Postumus</a>, so named because he was born after Marcus Agrippa died. Shortly after the Second Settlement, Agrippa was granted a five-year term of administering the eastern half of the Empire with the <i>imperium</i> of a proconsul and the same <i>tribunicia potestas</i> granted to Augustus (although not trumping Augustus's authority), his seat of governance stationed at <a href="/wiki/Samos" title="Samos">Samos</a> in the eastern <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen200544_229-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen200544-229">[218]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200358_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200358-230">[219]</a></sup> This granting of power showed Augustus's favor for Agrippa, but it was also a measure to please members of his Caesarian party by allowing one of their members to share a considerable amount of power with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200358_230-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200358-230">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p>Augustus's intent became apparent to make Gaius and Lucius Caesar his heirs when he adopted them as his own children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESyme1939416–417_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESyme1939416–417-231">[220]</a></sup> He took the consulship in 5 and 2 BC so that he could personally usher them into their political careers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScullard1982217_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScullard1982217-232">[221]</a></sup> and they were nominated for the consulships of AD 1 and 4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESyme1939417_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESyme1939417-233">[222]</a></sup> Augustus also showed favor to his stepsons, Livia's children from her first marriage <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nero_Claudius_Drusus_Germanicus" title="Nero Claudius Drusus Germanicus">Nero Claudius Drusus Germanicus</a> (henceforth referred to as Drusus) and <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius Claudius</a> (henceforth Tiberius), granting them military commands and public office, though seeming to favor Drusus. After Agrippa died in 12 BC, Tiberius was ordered to divorce his own wife <a href="/wiki/Vipsania_Agrippina" title="Vipsania Agrippina">Vipsania Agrippina</a> and marry Agrippa's widow, Augustus's daughter Julia—as soon as a period of mourning for Agrippa had ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003116_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003116-234">[223]</a></sup> Drusus's marriage to Augustus's niece <a href="/wiki/Antonia_Minor" title="Antonia Minor">Antonia</a> was considered an unbreakable affair, whereas Vipsania was "only" the daughter of the late Agrippa from his first marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003116_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003116-234">[223]</a></sup>
