</p><p>Tiberius shared in Augustus's tribune powers as of 6 BC, but shortly thereafter went into retirement, reportedly wanting no further role in politics while he exiled himself to <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003117_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003117-190">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen200546_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen200546-235">[224]</a></sup> No specific reason is known for his departure, though it could have been a combination of reasons, including a failing marriage with Julia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003117_190-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003117-190">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen200546_235-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen200546-235">[224]</a></sup> as well as a sense of envy and exclusion over Augustus's apparent favouring of his young grandchildren-turned-sons Gaius and Lucius. (Gaius and Lucius joined the college of priests at an early age, were presented to spectators in a more favorable light, and were introduced to the army in Gaul.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003117–118_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003117–118-236">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen200546–47_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen200546–47-237">[226]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the early deaths of both Lucius and Gaius in AD 2 and 4 respectively, and the earlier death of his brother Drusus (9 BC), Tiberius was recalled to Rome in June AD 4, where he was adopted by Augustus on the condition that he, in turn, adopt his nephew <a href="/wiki/Germanicus" title="Germanicus">Germanicus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003119_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003119-238">[227]</a></sup> This continued the tradition of presenting at least two generations of heirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003116_234-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003116-234">[223]</a></sup> In that year, Tiberius was also granted the powers of a tribune and proconsul, emissaries from foreign kings had to pay their respects to him, and by AD 13 was awarded with his second triumph and equal level of <i>imperium</i> with that of Augustus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003119–120_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003119–120-239">[228]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Cameo_of_France_CdM_Paris_Bab264_white_background.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2650" data-file-width="2450" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Great_Cameo_of_France_CdM_Paris_Bab264_white_background.jpg/220px-Great_Cameo_of_France_CdM_Paris_Bab264_white_background.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Great_Cameo_of_France_CdM_Paris_Bab264_white_background.jpg/330px-Great_Cameo_of_France_CdM_Paris_Bab264_white_background.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Great_Cameo_of_France_CdM_Paris_Bab264_white_background.jpg/440px-Great_Cameo_of_France_CdM_Paris_Bab264_white_background.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Cameo_of_France_CdM_Paris_Bab264_white_background.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The deified Augustus hovers over Tiberius and other Julio-Claudians in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Cameo_of_France" title="Great Cameo of France">Great Cameo of France</a></div></div></div>
<p>The only other possible claimant as heir was Postumus Agrippa, who had been exiled by Augustus in AD 7, his banishment made permanent by senatorial decree, and Augustus officially disowned him. He certainly fell out of Augustus's favor as an heir; the historian <a href="/wiki/Erich_S._Gruen" title="Erich S. Gruen">Erich S. Gruen</a> notes various contemporary sources that state Postumus Agrippa was a "vulgar young man, brutal and brutish, and of depraved character".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruen200549_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruen200549-240">[229]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 19 August AD 14,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[231]</a></sup> Augustus died while visiting <a href="/wiki/Nola" title="Nola">Nola</a> where his father had died. Both Tacitus and Cassius Dio wrote that Livia was rumored to have brought about Augustus's death by poisoning fresh figs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETacitus1.5_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETacitus1.5-243">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassius_Dio55.22.2,_56.30_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassius_Dio55.22.2,_56.30-244">[233]</a></sup> This element features in many modern works of historical fiction pertaining to Augustus's life, but some historians view it as likely to have been a salacious fabrication made by those who had favoured Postumus as heir, or other of Tiberius's political enemies. Livia had long been the target of similar rumors of poisoning on the behalf of her son, most or all of which are unlikely to have been true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEveritt2006312–320_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEveritt2006312–320-245">[234]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alternatively, it is possible that Livia did supply a poisoned fig (she did cultivate a variety of fig named for her that Augustus is said to have enjoyed), but did so as a means of assisted suicide rather than murder. Augustus's health had been in decline in the months immediately before his death, and he had made significant preparations for a smooth transition in power, having at last reluctantly settled on Tiberius as his choice of heir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003123_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003123-246">[235]</a></sup> It is likely that Augustus was not expected to return alive from Nola, but it seems that his health improved once there; it has therefore been speculated that Augustus and Livia conspired to end his life at the anticipated time, having committed all political process to accepting Tiberius, in order to not endanger that transition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEveritt2006312–320_245-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEveritt2006312–320-245">[234]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Photographs_of_the_Mausoleum_of_Augustus_14_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2268" data-file-width="3176" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Photographs_of_the_Mausoleum_of_Augustus_14_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Photographs_of_the_Mausoleum_of_Augustus_14_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Photographs_of_the_Mausoleum_of_Augustus_14_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Photographs_of_the_Mausoleum_of_Augustus_14_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Photographs_of_the_Mausoleum_of_Augustus_14_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Photographs_of_the_Mausoleum_of_Augustus_14_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Photographs_of_the_Mausoleum_of_Augustus_14_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum_of_Augustus" title="Mausoleum of Augustus">Mausoleum of Augustus</a> restored, 2021</div></div></div>
<p>Augustus's famous last words were, "Have I played the part well? Then applaud as I exit" (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">"Acta est fabula, plaudite"</i></span>)—referring to the play-acting and regal authority that he had put on as emperor. Publicly, though, his last words were, "Behold, I found Rome of clay, and leave her to you of marble" (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">"Marmoream se relinquere, quam latericiam accepisset"</i></span>). An enormous funerary procession of mourners traveled with Augustus's body from Nola to Rome, and on the day of his burial all public and private businesses closed for the day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003123_246-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003123-246">[235]</a></sup> Tiberius and his son Drusus delivered the eulogy while standing atop two <i><a href="/wiki/Rostra" title="Rostra">rostra</a></i>. Augustus's body was coffin-bound and cremated on a pyre close to <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum_of_Augustus" title="Mausoleum of Augustus">his mausoleum</a>. It was proclaimed that Augustus joined the company of the gods as a member of the Roman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantheon_(gods)" title="Pantheon (gods)">pantheon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003124_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003124-247">[236]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historian D. C. A. Shotter states that Augustus's policy of favoring the Julian family line over the Claudian might have afforded Tiberius sufficient cause to show open disdain for Augustus after the latter's death; instead, Tiberius was always quick to rebuke those who criticized Augustus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShotter1966210–212_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShotter1966210–212-248">[237]</a></sup> Shotter suggests that Augustus's deification obliged Tiberius to suppress any open resentment that he might have harbored, coupled with Tiberius's "extremely conservative" attitude towards religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShotter1966211_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShotter1966211-249">[238]</a></sup> Also, historian R. Shaw-Smith points to letters of Augustus to Tiberius which display affection towards Tiberius and high regard for his military merits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaw-Smith1971213_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaw-Smith1971213-250">[239]</a></sup> Shotter states that Tiberius focused his anger and criticism on <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Asinius_Gallus" title="Gaius Asinius Gallus">Gaius Asinius Gallus</a> (for marrying Vipsania after Augustus forced Tiberius to divorce her), as well as toward the two young Caesars, Gaius and Lucius—instead of Augustus, the real architect of his divorce and imperial demotion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShotter1966211_249-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShotter1966211-249">[238]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_Augustus" title="Cultural depictions of Augustus">Cultural depictions of Augustus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Folio_22r_-_The_Virgin,_the_Sibyl_and_the_Emperor_Augustus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1692" data-file-width="1110" decoding="async" height="335" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Folio_22r_-_The_Virgin%2C_the_Sibyl_and_the_Emperor_Augustus.jpg/220px-Folio_22r_-_The_Virgin%2C_the_Sibyl_and_the_Emperor_Augustus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Folio_22r_-_The_Virgin%2C_the_Sibyl_and_the_Emperor_Augustus.jpg/330px-Folio_22r_-_The_Virgin%2C_the_Sibyl_and_the_Emperor_Augustus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Folio_22r_-_The_Virgin%2C_the_Sibyl_and_the_Emperor_Augustus.jpg/440px-Folio_22r_-_The_Virgin%2C_the_Sibyl_and_the_Emperor_Augustus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Folio_22r_-_The_Virgin,_the_Sibyl_and_the_Emperor_Augustus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Virgin Mary and Child, the prophetess <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Vesta,_Tivoli" title="Temple of Vesta, Tivoli">Sibyl Tivoli</a> bottom left and the emperor Augustus in the bottom right, from the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_duc_de_Berry" title="Très Riches Heures du duc de Berry">Très Riches Heures du duc de Berry</a></i>. The likeness of Augustus is that of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_emperor" title="Byzantine emperor">Byzantine emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Manuel_II_Palaiologos" title="Manuel II Palaiologos">Manuel II Palaiologos</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[240]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Augustus_kameo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="511" data-file-width="449" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Augustus_kameo.jpg/220px-Augustus_kameo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Augustus_kameo.jpg/330px-Augustus_kameo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Augustus_kameo.jpg/440px-Augustus_kameo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Augustus_kameo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Augustus cameo at the center of the Medieval <a href="/wiki/Cross_of_Lothair" title="Cross of Lothair">Cross of Lothair</a></div></div></div>
<p>Augustus's reign laid the foundations of a regime that lasted, in one form or another, for nearly fifteen hundred years through the ultimate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Decline of the Roman Empire">decline of the Western Roman Empire</a> and until the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Fall of Constantinople</a> in 1453. Both his adoptive surname, Caesar, and his title <i>Augustus</i> became the permanent titles of the rulers of the Roman Empire for fourteen centuries after his death, in use both at <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Old Rome</a> and at <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">New Rome</a>. In many languages, <i>Caesar</i> became the word for <i>emperor</i>, as in the German <i><a href="/wiki/Kaiser" title="Kaiser">Kaiser</a></i> and in the Bulgarian and subsequently Russian <i><a href="/wiki/Tsar" title="Tsar">Tsar</a></i> (sometimes <i>Csar</i> or <i>Czar</i>). The cult of <i>Divus Augustus</i> continued until the state religion of the Empire was changed to <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> in 391 by <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a>. Consequently, there are many excellent statues and busts of the first emperor. He had composed an account of his achievements, the <i><a href="/wiki/Res_Gestae_Divi_Augusti" title="Res Gestae Divi Augusti">Res Gestae Divi Augusti</a></i>, to be inscribed in bronze in front of <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum_of_Augustus" title="Mausoleum of Augustus">his mausoleum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsSuetonius12CaesarsAugustushtml1014_101.4]_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsSuetonius12CaesarsAugustushtml1014_101.4]-252">[241]</a></sup> Copies of the text were inscribed throughout the Empire upon his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20031–2_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20031–2-253">[242]</a></sup> The inscriptions in Latin featured translations in Greek beside it, and were inscribed on many public edifices, such as the temple in <a href="/wiki/Ankara" title="Ankara">Ankara</a> dubbed the <i>Monumentum Ancyranum</i>, called the "queen of inscriptions" by historian <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Mommsen" title="Theodor Mommsen">Theodor Mommsen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20032_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20032-254">[243]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Res Gestae</i> is the only work to have survived from antiquity, though Augustus is also known to have composed poems entitled <i>Sicily</i>, <i>Epiphanus</i>, and <i>Ajax</i>, an autobiography of 13 books, a philosophical treatise, and a written rebuttal to Brutus's <i>Eulogy of Cato</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson199447_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson199447-255">[244]</a></sup> Historians are able to analyze excerpts of letters penned by Augustus, preserved in other works, to others for additional facts or clues about his personal life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaw-Smith1971213_250-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaw-Smith1971213-250">[239]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBourne191853–66_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBourne191853–66-256">[245]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many consider Augustus to be Rome's greatest emperor; his policies certainly extended the Empire's life span and initiated the celebrated <i>Pax Romana</i> or <i>Pax Augusta</i>. The Roman Senate wished subsequent emperors to "<a href="/wiki/Felicior_Augusto,_melior_Traiano" title="Felicior Augusto, melior Traiano">be more fortunate than Augustus and better than Trajan</a>". Augustus was intelligent, decisive, and a shrewd politician, but he was not perhaps as charismatic as Julius Caesar and was influenced on occasion by Livia (sometimes for the worse). Nevertheless, his legacy proved more enduring. The city of Rome was utterly transformed under Augustus, with Rome's first institutionalized <a href="/wiki/History_of_criminal_justice" title="History of criminal justice">police force</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fire_fighting" title="Fire fighting">fire fighting</a> force, and the establishment of the municipal <a href="/wiki/Praefectus" title="Praefectus">prefect</a> as a permanent office. The police force was divided into cohorts of 500 men each, while the units of firemen ranged from 500 to 1,000 men each, with 7 units assigned to 14 divided city sectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200379_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200379-257">[246]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Praefectus_vigilum" title="Praefectus vigilum">praefectus vigilum</a></i>, or "Prefect of the Watch" was put in charge of the <a href="/wiki/Vigiles" title="Vigiles">vigiles</a>, Rome's fire brigade and police.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson1994345_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson1994345-258">[247]</a></sup> With Rome's civil wars at an end, Augustus was also able to create a <a href="/wiki/Standing_army" title="Standing army">standing army</a> for the Roman Empire, fixed at a size of 28 legions of about 170,000 soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200385–87_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200385–87-259">[248]</a></sup> This was supported by numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auxiliaries_(Roman_military)" title="Auxiliaries (Roman military)">auxiliary</a> units of 500 non-citizen soldiers each, often recruited from recently conquered areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200386_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200386-260">[249]</a></sup>
</p><p>With his finances securing the maintenance of roads throughout Italy, Augustus also installed an official <a href="/wiki/Courier" title="Courier">courier</a> system of relay stations overseen by a military officer known as the <i>praefectus vehiculorum</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200381_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200381-261">[250]</a></sup> Besides the advent of swifter communication among Italian polities, his extensive building of roads throughout Italy also allowed Rome's armies to march swiftly and at an unprecedented pace across the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholmFerguson1981122_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholmFerguson1981122-262">[251]</a></sup> In the year 6 Augustus established the <i><a href="/wiki/Aerarium_militare" title="Aerarium militare">aerarium militare</a></i>, donating 170 million sesterces to the new military treasury that provided for both active and retired soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson19946_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson19946-263">[252]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the most enduring institutions of Augustus was the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_Guard" title="Praetorian Guard">Praetorian Guard</a> in 27 BC, originally a personal bodyguard unit on the battlefield that evolved into an imperial guard as well as an important political force in Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson1994341_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson1994341-264">[253]</a></sup>They had the power to intimidate the Senate, install new emperors, and depose ones they disliked; the last emperor they served was <a href="/wiki/Maxentius" title="Maxentius">Maxentius</a>, as it was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">Constantine I</a> who disbanded them in the early 4th century and destroyed their barracks, the <a href="/wiki/Castra_Praetoria" title="Castra Praetoria">Castra Praetoria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson1994341,_342_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson1994341,_342-265">[254]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Augustus-in-Kalabsha.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2016" data-file-width="1146" decoding="async" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Augustus-in-Kalabsha.jpg/170px-Augustus-in-Kalabsha.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Augustus-in-Kalabsha.jpg/255px-Augustus-in-Kalabsha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Augustus-in-Kalabsha.jpg/340px-Augustus-in-Kalabsha.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Augustus-in-Kalabsha.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Augustus as <a href="/wiki/Roman_pharaoh" title="Roman pharaoh">Roman pharaoh</a> in an Egyptian-style depiction, a stone carving of the <a href="/wiki/New_Kalabsha" title="New Kalabsha">Kalabsha Temple</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a></div></div></div>
<p>Although the most powerful individual in the Roman Empire, Augustus wished to embody the spirit of Republican virtue and norms. He also wanted to relate to and connect with the concerns of the plebs and lay people. He achieved this through various means of generosity and a cutting back of lavish excess. In the year 29 BC, Augustus gave 400 sesterces (equal to 1/10 of a Roman pound of gold) each to 250,000 citizens, 1,000 sesterces each to 120,000 veterans in the colonies, and spent 700 million sesterces in purchasing land for his soldiers to settle upon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200523_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200523-266">[255]</a></sup> He also restored 82 different temples to display his care for the <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman pantheon</a> of deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200523_266-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200523-266">[255]</a></sup> In 28 BC, he melted down 80 silver statues erected in his likeness and in honor of him, an attempt of his to appear frugal and modest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200523_266-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200523-266">[255]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KujulaKadphisesCoinAugustusImitation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1378" data-file-width="1416" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/KujulaKadphisesCoinAugustusImitation.jpg/220px-KujulaKadphisesCoinAugustusImitation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/KujulaKadphisesCoinAugustusImitation.jpg/330px-KujulaKadphisesCoinAugustusImitation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/KujulaKadphisesCoinAugustusImitation.jpg/440px-KujulaKadphisesCoinAugustusImitation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KujulaKadphisesCoinAugustusImitation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coin of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kushan" title="Kushan">Kushan</a> ruler <a href="/wiki/Kujula_Kadphises" title="Kujula Kadphises">Kujula Kadphises</a>, in the style of Roman emperor Augustus. <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>. AE dichalkon, <a href="/wiki/Tashkent" title="Tashkent">Chach</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> first half of 1st. Century, Weight:3.26 gm., Diam:18 mm. Caption: obverse in Greek ΚΟΖΟΛΑ ΚΑΔΑΦΕΣ ΧΟΡΑΝΟΥ ΖΑΟΟΥ, reverse in Kharoshti.</div></div></div>
<p>The longevity of Augustus's reign and its legacy to the Roman world should not be overlooked as a key factor in its success. As <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a> wrote, the younger generations alive in AD 14 had never known any form of government other than the Principate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETacitus[[s:The_Annals_(Tacitus)/Book_1#3|I.3]]_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETacitus[[s:The_Annals_(Tacitus)/Book_1#3|I.3]]-267">[256]</a></sup> Had Augustus died earlier (in 23 BC, for instance), matters might have turned out differently. The attrition of the civil wars on the old Republican oligarchy and the longevity of Augustus, therefore, must be seen as major contributing factors in the transformation of the Roman state into a <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> monarchy in these years. Augustus's own experience, his patience, his tact, and his political acumen also played their parts. He directed the future of the Empire down many lasting paths, from the existence of a standing professional army stationed at or near the frontiers, to the dynastic principle so often employed in the imperial succession, to the embellishment of the capital at the emperor's expense. Augustus's ultimate legacy was the peace and prosperity the Empire enjoyed for the next two centuries under the system he initiated. His memory was enshrined in the political ethos of the Imperial age as a paradigm of the good emperor. Every emperor of Rome adopted his name, Caesar Augustus, which gradually lost its character as a name and eventually became a title.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003124_247-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003124-247">[236]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Augustan_poetry" title="Augustan poetry">Augustan era poets</a> Virgil and Horace praised Augustus as a defender of Rome, an upholder of moral justice, and an individual who bore the brunt of responsibility in maintaining the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelsall1976120_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelsall1976120-268">[257]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, for his rule of Rome and establishing the principate, Augustus has also been subjected to criticism throughout the ages. The contemporary Roman jurist <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Antistius_Labeo" title="Marcus Antistius Labeo">Marcus Antistius Labeo</a> (d. AD 10/11), fond of the days of pre-Augustan republican <a href="/wiki/Liberty" title="Liberty">liberty</a> in which he had been born, openly criticized the Augustan regime. In the beginning of his <i><a href="/wiki/Annals_(Tacitus)" title="Annals (Tacitus)">Annals</a></i>, the Roman historian <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 56</span>–<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 117</span>) wrote that Augustus had cunningly subverted Republican Rome into a position of slavery. He continued to say that, with Augustus's death and swearing of loyalty to Tiberius, the people of Rome simply traded one slaveholder for another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStarr19525_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStarr19525-269">[258]</a></sup> Tacitus, however, records two contradictory but common views of Augustus:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Intelligent people praised or criticized him in varying ways. One opinion was as follows. Filial duty and a national emergency, in which there was no place for law-abiding conduct, had driven him to civil war—and this can neither be initiated nor maintained by decent methods. He had made many concessions to Anthony and to Lepidus for the sake of vengeance on his father's murderers. When Lepidus grew old and lazy, and Anthony's self-indulgence got the better of him, the only possible cure for the distracted country had been government by one man. However, Augustus had put the state in order not by making himself king or dictator, but by creating the Principate. The Empire's frontiers were on the ocean, or distant rivers. Armies, provinces, fleets, the whole system was interrelated. Roman citizens were protected by the law. Provincials were decently treated. Rome itself had been lavishly beautified. Force had been sparingly used—merely to preserve peace for the majority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETacitusI_9_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETacitusI_9-270">[259]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Augustus_Bronze_X23322_NAMAthens.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3486" data-file-width="3056" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Augustus_Bronze_X23322_NAMAthens.jpg/170px-Augustus_Bronze_X23322_NAMAthens.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Augustus_Bronze_X23322_NAMAthens.jpg/255px-Augustus_Bronze_X23322_NAMAthens.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Augustus_Bronze_X23322_NAMAthens.jpg/340px-Augustus_Bronze_X23322_NAMAthens.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Augustus_Bronze_X23322_NAMAthens.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fragment of a bronze equestrian statue of Augustus, 1st century AD, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Archaeological_Museum_of_Athens" title="National Archaeological Museum of Athens">National Archaeological Museum of Athens</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VirgilAeneidVI.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4231" data-file-width="4613" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/VirgilAeneidVI.jpg/220px-VirgilAeneidVI.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/VirgilAeneidVI.jpg/330px-VirgilAeneidVI.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/VirgilAeneidVI.jpg/440px-VirgilAeneidVI.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VirgilAeneidVI.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Virgil reading the Aeneid to Augustus and Octavia</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Joseph_Taillasson" title="Jean-Joseph Taillasson">Jean-Joseph Taillasson</a>, 1787</div></div></div>
<p>According to the second opposing opinion:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>filial duty and national crisis had been merely pretexts. In actual fact, the motive of Octavian, the future Augustus, was lust for power ... There had certainly been peace, but it was a blood-stained peace of disasters and assassinations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[260]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In a 2006 biography on Augustus, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Everitt" title="Anthony Everitt">Anthony Everitt</a> asserts that through the centuries, judgments on Augustus's reign have oscillated between these two extremes but stresses that:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Opposites do not have to be mutually exclusive, and we are not obliged to choose one or the other. The story of his career shows that Augustus was indeed ruthless, cruel, and ambitious for himself. This was only in part a personal trait, for upper-class Romans were educated to compete with one another and to excel. However, he combined an overriding concern for his personal interests with a deep-seated patriotism, based on a nostalgia of Rome's antique virtues. In his capacity as <i>princeps</i>, selfishness and selflessness coexisted in his mind. While fighting for dominance, he paid little attention to legality or to the normal civilities of political life. He was devious, untrustworthy, and bloodthirsty. But once he had established his authority, he governed efficiently and justly, generally allowed freedom of speech, and promoted the rule of law. He was immensely hardworking and tried as hard as any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Democracy_in_Ancient_Rome" title="Democracy in Ancient Rome">democratic</a> parliamentarian to treat his senatorial colleagues with respect and sensitivity. He suffered from no delusions of grandeur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEveritt2006324–325_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEveritt2006324–325-272">[261]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Tacitus was of the belief that <a href="/wiki/Nerva" title="Nerva">Nerva</a> (r. 96–98) successfully "mingled two formerly alien ideas, principate and liberty".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStarr19526_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStarr19526-273">[262]</a></sup> The 3rd-century historian Cassius Dio acknowledged Augustus as a benign, moderate ruler, yet like most other historians after the death of Augustus, Dio viewed Augustus as an <a href="/wiki/Autocracy" title="Autocracy">autocrat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStarr19525_269-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStarr19525-269">[258]</a></sup> The poet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Annaeus_Lucanus" title="Marcus Annaeus Lucanus">Marcus Annaeus Lucanus</a> (AD 39–65) was of the opinion that Caesar's victory over Pompey and the fall of <a href="/wiki/Cato_the_Younger" title="Cato the Younger">Cato the Younger</a> (95 BC–46 BC) marked the end of traditional liberty in Rome; historian <a href="/wiki/Chester_Starr" title="Chester Starr">Chester Starr</a> writes of his avoidance of criticizing Augustus, "perhaps Augustus was too sacred a figure to accuse directly."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStarr19526_273-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStarr19526-273">[262]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish" title="Anglo-Irish">Anglo-Irish</a> writer <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Swift" title="Jonathan Swift">Jonathan Swift</a> (1667–1745), in his <i>Discourse on the Contests and Dissentions in Athens and Rome</i>, criticized Augustus for installing tyranny over Rome, and likened what he believed <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">Great Britain</a>'s virtuous <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a> to Rome's moral Republic of the 2nd century BC. In his criticism of Augustus, the admiral and historian <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Gordon_(Royal_Scots_Navy_officer)" title="Thomas Gordon (Royal Scots Navy officer)">Thomas Gordon</a> (1658–1741) compared Augustus to the puritanical tyrant <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> (1599–1658).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelsall1976118_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelsall1976118-274">[263]</a></sup> Thomas Gordon and the French political philosopher <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a> (1689–1755) both remarked that Augustus was a coward in battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelsall1976119_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelsall1976119-275">[264]</a></sup> In his <i>Memoirs of the Court of Augustus</i>, the Scottish scholar <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Blackwell_(scholar)" title="Thomas Blackwell (scholar)">Thomas Blackwell</a> (1701–1757) deemed Augustus a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Machiavelli" title="Machiavelli">Machiavellian</a> ruler, "a bloodthirsty vindicative usurper", "wicked and worthless", "a mean spirit", and a "tyrant".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelsall1976119_275-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelsall1976119-275">[264]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revenue_reforms">Revenue reforms</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AugustusCoinPudukottaiHoardIndia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1479" data-file-width="1496" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/AugustusCoinPudukottaiHoardIndia.jpg/220px-AugustusCoinPudukottaiHoardIndia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/AugustusCoinPudukottaiHoardIndia.jpg/330px-AugustusCoinPudukottaiHoardIndia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/AugustusCoinPudukottaiHoardIndia.jpg/440px-AugustusCoinPudukottaiHoardIndia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AugustusCoinPudukottaiHoardIndia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coin of Augustus found at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pudukottai" title="Pudukottai">Pudukottai</a> hoard, from an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Tamil_country" title="Ancient Tamil country">ancient Tamil country</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pandyan_Kingdom" title="Pandyan Kingdom">Pandyan Kingdom</a> of present-day <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a> in India, a testimony to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Roman_trade" title="Indo-Roman trade">Indo-Roman trade</a>. <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>. Caption: AVGVSTVS DIVI F[ILIVS]. (The vertical slice, not part of the original design, was likely an old test cut to make sure the coin was solid rather than a <a href="/wiki/Fourr%C3%A9e" title="Fourrée">fourrée</a>.)</div></div></div>
<p>Augustus's public <a href="/wiki/Revenue" title="Revenue">revenue</a> reforms had a great impact on the subsequent success of the Empire. Augustus brought a far greater portion of the Empire's expanded land base under consistent, direct taxation from Rome, instead of exacting varying, intermittent, and somewhat arbitrary tributes from each local province as Augustus's predecessors had done. This reform greatly increased Rome's net revenue from its territorial acquisitions, stabilized its flow, and regularized the financial relationship between Rome and the provinces, rather than provoking fresh resentments with each new arbitrary exaction of tribute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200383–84_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200383–84-276">[265]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HymiariteKingdomAugustusImitation1stCenturyCE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1670" data-file-width="1698" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/HymiariteKingdomAugustusImitation1stCenturyCE.jpg/220px-HymiariteKingdomAugustusImitation1stCenturyCE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/HymiariteKingdomAugustusImitation1stCenturyCE.jpg/330px-HymiariteKingdomAugustusImitation1stCenturyCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/HymiariteKingdomAugustusImitation1stCenturyCE.jpg/440px-HymiariteKingdomAugustusImitation1stCenturyCE.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HymiariteKingdomAugustusImitation1stCenturyCE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1st century coin of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Himyarite" title="Himyarite">Himyarite</a> Kingdom, southern coast of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabian_peninsula" title="Arabian peninsula">Arabian peninsula</a>. This is also an imitation of a coin of Augustus.</div></div></div>
<p>The measures of taxation in the reign of Augustus were determined by population census, with fixed quotas for each province. Citizens of Rome and Italy paid indirect taxes, while direct taxes were exacted from the provinces. Indirect taxes included a 4% tax on the price of slaves, a 1% tax on goods sold at auction, and a 5% tax on the inheritance of estates valued at over 100,000 sesterces by persons other than the <a href="/wiki/Next_of_kin" title="Next of kin">next of kin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson1994404_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson1994404-277">[266]</a></sup>
</p><p>An equally important reform was the abolition of private <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tax_farming" title="Tax farming">tax farming</a>, which was replaced by salaried civil service tax collectors. Private contractors who collected taxes for the State were the norm in the Republican era. Some of them were powerful enough to influence the number of votes for men running for offices in Rome. These tax farmers called <a href="/wiki/Publican" title="Publican">publicans</a> were infamous for their depredations, great private wealth, and the right to tax local areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200383–84_276-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200383–84-276">[265]</a></sup>
</p><p>The use of Egypt's immense land rents to finance the Empire's operations resulted from Augustus's conquest of Egypt and the shift to a Roman form of government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson1994144_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson1994144-278">[267]</a></sup> As it was effectively considered Augustus's private property rather than a province of the Empire, it became part of each succeeding emperor's patrimonium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson1994144–145_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson1994144–145-279">[268]</a></sup>
</p><p>Instead of a legate or proconsul, Augustus installed a prefect from the equestrian class to administer Egypt and maintain its lucrative seaports; this position became the highest political achievement for any equestrian besides becoming <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_prefect" title="Praetorian prefect">Prefect of the Praetorian Guard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson1994145_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson1994145-280">[269]</a></sup> The highly productive agricultural land of Egypt yielded enormous revenues that were available to Augustus and his successors to pay for public works and military expeditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson1994144_278-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson1994144-278">[267]</a></sup> During his reign the <a href="/wiki/Venatio" title="Venatio">circus games</a> resulted in the killing of 3,500 <a href="/wiki/North_African_elephant" title="North African elephant">elephants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woolf2007_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woolf2007-281">[270]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Month_of_August">Month of August</span></h3>
<p>The month of August (Latin: <i>Augustus</i>) is named after Augustus; until his time it was called <a href="/wiki/Sextilis" title="Sextilis">Sextilis</a> (named so because it had been the sixth month of the original <a href="/wiki/Roman_calendar" title="Roman calendar">Roman calendar</a> and the Latin word for six is <i>sex</i>). Commonly repeated lore has it that August has 31 days because Augustus wanted his month to match the length of Julius Caesar's July, but this is an invention of the 13th century scholar <a href="/wiki/Johannes_de_Sacrobosco" title="Johannes de Sacrobosco">Johannes de Sacrobosco</a>. Sextilis in fact had 31 days before it was renamed, and it was not chosen for its length (see <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a>).
</p><p>According to a <i>senatus consultum</i> quoted by <a href="/wiki/Macrobius" title="Macrobius">Macrobius</a>, Sextilis was renamed to honor Augustus because several of the most significant events in his rise to power, culminating in the fall of Alexandria, fell in that month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[271]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Creation_of_.22Italia.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='Creation_of_"Italia"'>Creation of "Italia"</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Roman_Italy" title="Roman Italy">Roman Italy</a> was established by Augustus in 7 BC with the Latin name "Italia". This was the first time that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_peninsula" title="Italian peninsula">Italian peninsula</a> was united administratively and politically under the same name. Due to this act, Augustus was called the <i>Father of Italy</i> by Italian historians such as G. Giannelli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[272]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Building_projects">Building projects</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main page: <a href="/wiki/Category:Augustan_building_projects" title="Category:Augustan building projects">Category:Augustan building projects</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Vitruvius" title="Vitruvius">Vitruvius</a> and <a href="/wiki/De_architectura" title="De architectura">De architectura</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RomaAraPacisDecorazioneVegetale.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="621" data-file-width="908" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/RomaAraPacisDecorazioneVegetale.jpg/220px-RomaAraPacisDecorazioneVegetale.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/RomaAraPacisDecorazioneVegetale.jpg/330px-RomaAraPacisDecorazioneVegetale.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/RomaAraPacisDecorazioneVegetale.jpg/440px-RomaAraPacisDecorazioneVegetale.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RomaAraPacisDecorazioneVegetale.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Close up on the sculpted detail of the <a href="/wiki/Ara_Pacis" title="Ara Pacis">Ara Pacis</a> (Altar of Peace), 13 BC to 9 BC</div></div></div>
<p>On his deathbed, Augustus boasted "I found a Rome of bricks; I leave to you one of marble." Although there is some truth in the literal meaning of this, Cassius Dio asserts that it was a metaphor for the Empire's strength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dio_56.30.3_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-dio_56.30.3-284">[273]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marble" title="Marble">Marble</a> could be found in buildings of Rome before Augustus, but it was not extensively used as a building material until the reign of Augustus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson199434_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson199434-285">[274]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although this did not apply to the <a href="/wiki/Suburra" title="Suburra">Subura</a> slums, which were still as rickety and fire-prone as ever, he did leave a mark on the monumental topography of the centre and of the <a href="/wiki/Campus_Martius" title="Campus Martius">Campus Martius</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Ara_Pacis" title="Ara Pacis">Ara Pacis</a> (Altar of Peace) and monumental sundial, whose central <a href="/wiki/Gnomon" title="Gnomon">gnomon</a> was an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obelisks_of_Rome" title="Obelisks of Rome">obelisk</a> taken from Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003122_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003122-286">[275]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">relief</a> sculptures decorating the Ara Pacis visually augmented the written record of Augustus's triumphs in the <i>Res Gestae</i>. Its reliefs depicted the imperial pageants of the <a href="/wiki/Praetor" title="Praetor">praetorians</a>, the Vestals, and the citizenry of Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson199432_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson199432-287">[276]</a></sup>
</p><p>He also built the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Caesar" title="Temple of Caesar">Temple of Caesar</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Baths_of_Agrippa" title="Baths of Agrippa">Baths of Agrippa</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Forum_of_Augustus" title="Forum of Augustus">Forum of Augustus</a> with its <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Mars_Ultor" title="Temple of Mars Ultor">Temple of Mars Ultor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERes_Gestae_Divi_Augusti_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERes_Gestae_Divi_Augusti-288">[277]</a></sup> Other projects were either encouraged by him, such as the <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_Balbus" title="Theatre of Balbus">Theatre of Balbus</a>, and Agrippa's construction of the <a href="/wiki/Pantheon,_Rome" title="Pantheon, Rome">Pantheon</a>, or funded by him in the name of others, often relations (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Porticus_Octaviae" title="Porticus Octaviae">Portico of Octavia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_Marcellus" title="Theatre of Marcellus">Theatre of Marcellus</a>). Even his <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum_of_Augustus" title="Mausoleum of Augustus">Mausoleum of Augustus</a> was built before his death to house members of his family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003118–121_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003118–121-289">[278]</a></sup> To celebrate his victory at the Battle of Actium, the <a href="/wiki/Arch_of_Augustus,_Rome" title="Arch of Augustus, Rome">Arch of Augustus</a> was built in 29 BC near the entrance of the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Castor_and_Pollux" title="Temple of Castor and Pollux">Temple of Castor and Pollux</a>, and widened in 19 BC to include a triple-arch design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson199434_285-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson199434-285">[274]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vienne_-_Temple_d%27Auguste_et_de_Livie_-1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2817" data-file-width="3059" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Vienne_-_Temple_d%27Auguste_et_de_Livie_-1.jpg/220px-Vienne_-_Temple_d%27Auguste_et_de_Livie_-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Vienne_-_Temple_d%27Auguste_et_de_Livie_-1.jpg/330px-Vienne_-_Temple_d%27Auguste_et_de_Livie_-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Vienne_-_Temple_d%27Auguste_et_de_Livie_-1.jpg/440px-Vienne_-_Temple_d%27Auguste_et_de_Livie_-1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vienne_-_Temple_d%27Auguste_et_de_Livie_-1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Augustus_and_Livia" title="Temple of Augustus and Livia">Temple of Augustus and Livia</a> in <a href="/wiki/Vienne,_Is%C3%A8re" title="Vienne, Isère">Vienne</a>, late 1st century BC</div></div></div>
<p>After the death of Agrippa in 12 BC, a solution had to be found in maintaining Rome's water supply system. This came about because it was overseen by Agrippa when he served as aedile, and was even funded by him afterwards when he was a private citizen paying at his own expense. In that year, Augustus arranged a system where the Senate designated three of its members as prime commissioners in charge of the water supply and to ensure that Rome's aqueducts did not fall into disrepair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200379_257-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200379-257">[246]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late Augustan era, the commission of five senators called the <i>curatores locorum publicorum iudicandorum</i> (translated as "Supervisors of Public Property") was put in charge of maintaining public buildings and temples of the state cult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200379_257-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200379-257">[246]</a></sup> Augustus created the senatorial group of the <i>curatores viarum</i> (translated as "Supervisors for Roads") for the upkeep of roads; this senatorial commission worked with local officials and contractors to organize regular repairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200381_261-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200381-261">[250]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_order" title="Corinthian order">Corinthian order</a> of architectural style originating from ancient Greece was the dominant architectural style in the age of Augustus and the imperial phase of Rome. Suetonius once commented that Rome was unworthy of its status as an imperial capital, yet Augustus and Agrippa set out to dismantle this sentiment by transforming the appearance of Rome upon the classical Greek model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson199434_285-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson199434-285">[274]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Residences">Residences</span></h4>
<p>The official residence of Augustus was the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domus_Augusti" title="Domus Augusti">Domus Augusti</a></i> on the Palatine which he made into a palace after buying it in 41/40 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_72_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_72-290">[279]</a></sup> He had other residences such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Gardens_of_Maecenas" title="Gardens of Maecenas">horti maecenati</a></i> in Rome where Augustus preferred to stay whenever he became ill and which Maecenas left to him in his will in 8 BC.  The great villa of <a href="/wiki/Vedius_Pollio" title="Vedius Pollio">Vedius Pollio</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Posilipo" title="Posilipo">Posilipo</a> near Naples was beqeathed (probably forced) to him in 15 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassius_DioBook_LIV_23_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassius_DioBook_LIV_23-291">[280]</a></sup>
</p><p>Augustus built the <a href="/wiki/Palazzo_a_Mare" title="Palazzo a Mare">Palazzo a Mare</a> palace on <a href="/wiki/Capri" title="Capri">Capri</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[281]</a></sup> He also built the immense <a href="/wiki/Villa_Giulia_(Naples)" title="Villa Giulia (Naples)">Villa Giulia</a> on the island of <a href="/wiki/Ventotene" title="Ventotene">Ventotene</a> as a summer residence early in his reign. The family home of Augustus was probably the villa at <a href="/wiki/Somma_Vesuviana" title="Somma Vesuviana">Somma Vesuviana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nola" title="Nola">Nola</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[282]</a></sup> This was the location where he died and where his father also died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[283]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_appearance_and_official_images">Physical appearance and official images</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SFEC_BritMus_Roman_Modification1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1282" data-file-width="981" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/SFEC_BritMus_Roman_Modification1.jpg/170px-SFEC_BritMus_Roman_Modification1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/SFEC_BritMus_Roman_Modification1.jpg/255px-SFEC_BritMus_Roman_Modification1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/SFEC_BritMus_Roman_Modification1.jpg/340px-SFEC_BritMus_Roman_Modification1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SFEC_BritMus_Roman_Modification1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Mero%C3%AB_Head" title="Meroë Head">Meroë Head</a> of Augustus, bronze <a href="/wiki/Roman_portraiture" title="Roman portraiture">Roman portraiture</a> bust from <a href="/wiki/Mero%C3%AB" title="Meroë">Meroë</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Kingdom of Kush</a> (<a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a>, modern Sudan), 27–25 BC</div></div></div>
<p>His biographer <a href="/wiki/Suetonius" title="Suetonius">Suetonius</a>, writing about a century after Augustus's death, described his appearance as: "... unusually handsome and exceedingly graceful at all periods of his life, though he cared nothing for personal adornment. He was so far from being particular about the dressing of his hair, that he would have several barbers working in a hurry at the same time, and as for his beard he now had it clipped and now shaved, while at the very same time he would either be reading or writing something ... He had clear, bright eyes ... His teeth were wide apart, small, and ill-kept; his hair was slightly curly and inclined to golden;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[l]</a></sup> his eyebrows met. His ears were of moderate size, and his nose projected a little at the top and then bent ever so slightly inward. His complexion was between dark and fair. He was short of stature, although Julius Marathus, his freedman and keeper of his records, says that he was five feet and nine inches (just under 5 ft. 7 in., or 1.70 meters, in modern height measurements), but this was concealed by the fine proportion and symmetry of his figure, and was noticeable only by comparison with some taller person standing beside him...",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsSuetonius12CaesarsAugustushtml79_79]_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsSuetonius12CaesarsAugustushtml79_79]-297">[285]</a></sup> adding that "his shoes [were] somewhat high-soled, to make him look taller than he really was".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsSuetonius12CaesarsAugustushtml73_73]_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuetonius''Augustus''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsSuetonius12CaesarsAugustushtml73_73]-298">[286]</a></sup> Scientific analysis of traces of paint found in his official statues show that he most likely had light brown hair and eyes (his hair and eyes were depicted as the same color).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[287]</a></sup>
</p><p>His official images were very tightly controlled and idealized, drawing from a tradition of <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_portraiture" title="Hellenistic portraiture">Hellenistic portraiture</a> rather than the tradition of realism in <a href="/wiki/Roman_portraiture" title="Roman portraiture">Roman portraiture</a>. He first appeared on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_coinage" title="Ancient Roman coinage">coins</a> at the age of 19, and from about 29 BC "the explosion in the number of Augustan portraits attests a concerted propaganda campaign aimed at dominating all aspects of civil, religious, economic and military life with Augustus's person."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[288]</a></sup> The early images did indeed depict a young man, but although there were gradual changes his images remained youthful until he died in his seventies, by which time they had "a distanced air of ageless majesty".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[289]</a></sup> Among the best known of many surviving portraits are the <a href="/wiki/Augustus_of_Prima_Porta" title="Augustus of Prima Porta">Augustus of Prima Porta</a>, the image on the <a href="/wiki/Ara_Pacis" title="Ara Pacis">Ara Pacis</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Via_Labicana_Augustus" title="Via Labicana Augustus">Via Labicana Augustus</a>, which depicts him in his role as <i><a href="/wiki/Pontifex_maximus" title="Pontifex maximus">pontifex maximus</a></i>. Several <a href="/wiki/Engraved_gem" title="Engraved gem">cameo portraits</a> include the <a href="/wiki/Blacas_Cameo" title="Blacas Cameo">Blacas Cameo</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Gemma_Augustea" title="Gemma Augustea">Gemma Augustea</a></i>.
</p>

<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">His full name was "Gaius Julius Caesar Augustus" according to <a href="/wiki/Roman_naming_conventions" title="Roman naming conventions">Roman naming conventions</a>. However, he officially called himself "Caesar Augustus".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESyme1958_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESyme1958-1">[1]</a></sup> See <a href="#Name">Name</a> for further details.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The dates of his rule are contemporary dates; Augustus lived under two calendars, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_calendar" title="Roman calendar">Roman Republican</a> until 45 BC and the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a> after 45 BC. Due to departures from <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a>'s intentions, Augustus finished restoring the Julian calendar in March AD 4 and the correspondence between the proleptic Julian calendar and the calendar observed in Rome is uncertain before 8 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlackburnHolford-Strevens2003670–671_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlackburnHolford-Strevens2003670–671-3">[2]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">Cassius Dio</a> instead gives him the name <b>Caepias</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassius_Dio''Book_45''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayereromantextscassius_dio45html1_1]_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassius_Dio''Book_45''_[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayereromantextscassius_dio45html1_1]-7">[5]</a></sup> probably a corruption of "Caesar".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">His daughter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julia_(daughter_of_Julius_Caesar)" title="Julia (daughter of Julius Caesar)">Julia</a> had died in 54 BC.; his son <a href="/wiki/Caesarion" title="Caesarion">Caesarion</a> by Cleopatra was not recognized by Roman law and was not mentioned in his will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suetonius_Julius_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Suetonius_Julius-33">[30]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-80">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Appian" title="Appian">Appian</a> estimates that 300 senators were proscribed, while his earlier contemporary <a href="/wiki/Livy" title="Livy">Livy</a> asserted that only 130 senators had been proscribed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESouthern199852–53_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESouthern199852–53-79">[75]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-94"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-94">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">These were <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Helios" title="Alexander Helios">Alexander Helios</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cleopatra_Selene_II" title="Cleopatra Selene II">Cleopatra Selene II</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_Philadelphus_(son_of_Cleopatra)" title="Ptolemy Philadelphus (son of Cleopatra)">Ptolemy Philadelphus</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-155"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-155">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ancient historians, however, often give him a rule of 56 years. None of them seem to agree on the exact start date, though.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[148]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-156"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-156">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">He was first proclaimed <i>imperator</i> on 16 April 43 BC, after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Forum_Gallorum" title="Battle of Forum Gallorum">Battle of Forum Gallorum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFishwick2004250_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFishwick2004250-66">[62]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-162"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-162">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">He acted on the orders of Marcellus and Augustus<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESouthern1998108_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESouthern1998108-160">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200355_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200355-161">[153]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-201"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-201">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The date is provided by inscribed calendars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowersock1990380_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowersock1990380-199">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[191]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-204"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-204">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dio reports this under 13 BC, probably as the year in which Lepidus died<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowersock1990383_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowersock1990383-202">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEder200528_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEder200528-203">[193]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-296"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-296">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Goldsworthy" title="Adrian Goldsworthy">Adrian Goldsworthy</a> such descriptions of color are hard to judge and may simply mean brown rather than black hair<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldsworthy201468_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldsworthy201468-295">[284]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-142"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-142">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Censorinus" title="Censorinus">Censorinus</a> <a class="external text" href="https://penelope.uchicago.edu/Thayer/L/Roman/Texts/Censorinus/text*.html#21.8" rel="nofollow">XXI.8</a> : "<i>quamvis ex ante diem XVI kal. Febr. imperator Caesar</i>". The number is right, but the phrasing is not.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-143"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-143">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fasti_Praenestini" title="Fasti Praenestini">Fasti Praenestini</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[133]</a></sup><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feriale_Cumanum" title="Feriale Cumanum">Feriale Cumanum</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[134]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovid%27s_Fasti" title="Ovid's Fasti">Ovid's Fasti</a> gives 13 January, the very same date in which the Senate powers were "restored".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[135]</a></sup> The 3rd century <a href="/wiki/Censorinus" title="Censorinus"><i>De die Natali</i></a> gives 17 January, a mistake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[136]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200350-144"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200350_144-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács200350_144-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFEckTakács2003">Eck &amp; Takács 2003</a>, p. 50.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003149-145"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács2003149_145-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFEckTakács2003">Eck &amp; Takács 2003</a>, p. 149.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20033,_149-147"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEckTakács20033,_149_147-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFEckTakács2003">Eck &amp; Takács 2003</a>, pp. 3, 149.</span>

<li id="cite_note-149"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-149">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFHammond1957"><a href="/wiki/Mason_Hammond" title="Mason Hammond">Hammond, Mason</a> (1957). <span class="cs1-lock-subscription" title="Paid subscription required"><a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/stable/4238646" rel="nofollow">"Imperial Elements in the Formula of the Roman Emperors during the First Two and a Half Centuries of the Empire"</a></span>. <i>Memoirs of the American Academy in Rome</i>. <b>25</b>: 29–31. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.2307%2F4238646" rel="nofollow">10.2307/4238646</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JSTOR_(identifier)" title="JSTOR (identifier)">JSTOR</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.jstor.org/stable/4238646" rel="nofollow">4238646</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Memoirs+of+the+American+Academy+in+Rome&amp;rft.atitle=Imperial+Elements+in+the+Formula+of+the+Roman+Emperors+during+the+First+Two+and+a+Half+Centuries+of+the+Empire&amp;rft.volume=25&amp;rft.pages=29-31&amp;rft.date=1957&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.2307%2F4238646&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F4238646%23id-name%3DJSTOR&amp;rft.aulast=Hammond&amp;rft.aufirst=Mason&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F4238646&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAugustus"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-150"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-150">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a> (1st century), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Jewish_War" title="The Jewish War">The Jewish War</a></i> <a class="external text" href="https://www.gutenberg.org/cache/epub/2850/pg2850-images.html" rel="nofollow">IX</a>. "Fifty-seven years, six months, and two days". 17 February 44 BC; 15 March is probably intended.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-151"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-151">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Suetonius" title="Suetonius">Suetonius</a> (121) <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_of_Augustus" title="Life of Augustus">Life of Augustus</a></i> 8., "With Antony alone for nearly twelve years, and finally by himself for forty-four." 56 years in total (from 43 BC).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-152"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-152">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Theophilus_of_Antioch" title="Theophilus of Antioch">Theophilus</a> (180–192), <i><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Page:Ante-Nicene_Christian_Library_Vol_3.djvu/142">To Autolycus</a></i> XXVII. "56 years 4 months 1 day". 18 April; referencing either his first acclamation as <i><a href="/wiki/Imperator" title="Imperator">imperator</a></i> (16 April) or his victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mutina" title="Battle of Mutina">Battle of Mutina</a> (21 April).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-153"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-153">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">Cassius Dio</a> (230). <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historia_Romana" title="Historia Romana">Historia Romana</a></i> 56, 5. "Forty-four years lacking thirteen days." Dio is one of the few writers that reckons from the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Actium" title="Battle of Actium">Battle of Actium</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-154"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-154">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> (4th century), <i><a href="/wiki/Chronicon_(Jerome)" title="Chronicon (Jerome)">Chronicon</a></i>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.attalus.org/translate/jerome2.html" rel="nofollow">184.2.</a> "56 years and 6 months." February/March 43 BC; this incorrect calculation is followed by most later historians.</span>
</li>
